<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Dinosaur_(disambiguation)" title="Dinosaur (disambiguation)">Dinosaur (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neognathae.jpg"><img alt="montage of four birds" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="968" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Neognathae.jpg/220px-Neognathae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Neognathae.jpg/330px-Neognathae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Neognathae.jpg/440px-Neognathae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neognathae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">Birds</a> are avian dinosaurs, and in <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a> are included in the group Dinosauria.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Dinosaurs</b> are a diverse group of <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cold-blooded_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-cold-blooded-7">[note 1]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> <b>Dinosauria</b>. They first appeared during the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_period" title="Geological period">period</a>, between 243 and 233.23 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Million_years_ago" title="Million years ago">million years ago</a> (mya), although the exact origin and timing of the <a href="#Evolutionary_history">evolution of dinosaurs</a> is the subject of active research. They became the dominant terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">Triassic–Jurassic extinction event</a> 201.3 mya; their dominance continued throughout the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> periods. The <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> record shows that <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> are <a href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaur" title="Feathered dinosaur">feathered dinosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_birds" title="Evolution of birds">having evolved</a> from earlier <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Jurassic" title="Late Jurassic">Late Jurassic</a> epoch, and are the only dinosaur lineage known to have survived the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> approximately 66 mya. Dinosaurs can therefore be divided into <b>avian dinosaurs</b>—birds—and the extinct <b>non-avian dinosaurs</b>, which are all dinosaurs other than birds.
</p><p>Dinosaurs are a varied group of animals from <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological" title="Ecological">ecological</a> standpoints. Birds, at over 10,700 living <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, are among the most diverse group of vertebrates. Using fossil evidence, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleontologists" title="Paleontologists">paleontologists</a> have identified over 900 distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">genera</a> and more than 1,000 different species of non-avian dinosaurs. Dinosaurs are represented on every continent by both <a href="/wiki/Neontology" title="Neontology">extant</a> species (birds) and fossil remains. Through the first half of the 20th century, before birds were recognized as dinosaurs, most of the scientific community believed dinosaurs to have been sluggish and <a href="/wiki/Poikilotherm" title="Poikilotherm">cold-blooded</a>. Most <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_renaissance" title="Dinosaur renaissance">research conducted since the 1970s</a>, however, has indicated that dinosaurs were active animals with elevated <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolisms</a> and numerous adaptations for social interaction. Some were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbivorous" title="Herbivorous">herbivorous</a>, others <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivorous" title="Carnivorous">carnivorous</a>. Evidence suggests that all dinosaurs were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg-laying" title="Egg-laying">egg-laying</a>, and that <a href="/wiki/Nest" title="Nest">nest</a>-building was a trait shared by many dinosaurs, both avian and non-avian.
</p><p>While dinosaurs were ancestrally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipedal" title="Bipedal">bipedal</a>, many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">extinct</a> groups included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadrupedal" title="Quadrupedal">quadrupedal</a> species, and some were able to shift between these stances. Elaborate display structures such as horns or crests are common to all dinosaur groups, and some extinct groups developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeletal" title="Skeletal">skeletal</a> modifications such as <a href="/wiki/Armour_(anatomy)" title="Armour (anatomy)">bony armor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spine_(zoology)" title="Spine (zoology)">spines</a>. While the dinosaurs' modern-day surviving avian lineage (birds) are generally small due to the constraints of flight, many prehistoric dinosaurs (non-avian and avian) were large-bodied—the largest <a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">sauropod</a> dinosaurs are estimated to have reached lengths of 39.7 meters (130 feet) and heights of 18 m (59 ft) and were the largest land animals of all time. The misconception that non-avian dinosaurs were uniformly gigantic is based in part on <a href="/wiki/Taphonomy" title="Taphonomy">preservation bias</a>, as large, sturdy <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a> are more likely to last until they are fossilized. Many dinosaurs were quite small, some measuring about 50 centimeters (20 inches) in length.
</p><p>The first dinosaur fossils were recognized in the early 19th century, with the name "dinosaur" (meaning "terrible lizard") being coined by Sir <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a> in 1841 to refer to these "great fossil lizards". Since then, mounted fossil dinosaur skeletons have been major attractions at museums worldwide, and dinosaurs have become an enduring part of popular culture. The large sizes of some dinosaurs, as well as their seemingly monstrous and fantastic nature, have ensured their regular appearance in best-selling books and films, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park" title="Jurassic Park">Jurassic Park</a></i>. Persistent public enthusiasm for the animals has resulted in significant funding for dinosaur science, and new discoveries are regularly covered by the media.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_nomenclature" title="Phylogenetic nomenclature">phylogenetic nomenclature</a>, dinosaurs are usually defined as the group consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">most recent common ancestor</a> (MRCA) of <i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Bird#Diversification_of_modern_birds" title="Bird">modern birds</a> (Neornithes), and all its descendants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB04dino_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-MJB04dino-8">[7]</a></sup> It has also been suggested that Dinosauria be defined with respect to the MRCA of <i><a href="/wiki/Megalosaurus" title="Megalosaurus">Megalosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Iguanodon" title="Iguanodon">Iguanodon</a></i>, because these were two of the three genera cited by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a> when he recognized the Dinosauria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olshevsky2000_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-olshevsky2000-9">[8]</a></sup> Both definitions result in the same set of animals being defined as dinosaurs: "Dinosauria = <a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">Ornithischia</a> + <a href="/wiki/Saurischia" title="Saurischia">Saurischia</a>". This definition includes major groups such as <a href="/wiki/Ankylosauria" title="Ankylosauria">ankylosaurians</a> (armored herbivorous quadrupeds), <a href="/wiki/Stegosauria" title="Stegosauria">stegosaurians</a> (plated herbivorous quadrupeds), <a href="/wiki/Ceratopsia" title="Ceratopsia">ceratopsians</a> (bipedal or quadrupedal herbivores with <a href="/wiki/Neck_frill" title="Neck frill">neck frills</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pachycephalosauria" title="Pachycephalosauria">pachycephalosaurians</a> (bipedal herbivores with thick skulls), <a href="/wiki/Ornithopoda" title="Ornithopoda">ornithopods</a> (bipedal or quadrupedal herbivores including "<a href="/wiki/Hadrosauridae" title="Hadrosauridae">duck-bills</a>"), <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a> (mostly bipedal carnivores and birds), and <a href="/wiki/Sauropodomorpha" title="Sauropodomorpha">sauropodomorphs</a> (mostly large herbivorous quadrupeds with long necks and tails).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letal05_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letal05-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Birds are now recognized as being the sole surviving lineage of theropod dinosaurs. In traditional <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a>, birds were considered a separate <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a> that had evolved from dinosaurs, a distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superorder" title="Superorder">superorder</a>. However, a majority of contemporary paleontologists concerned with dinosaurs reject the traditional style of classification in favor of <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> taxonomy; this approach requires that, for a group to be natural, all descendants of members of the group must be included in the group as well. Birds are thus considered to be dinosaurs and dinosaurs are, therefore, not extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> Birds are classified as belonging to the subgroup <a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">Maniraptora</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Coelurosauria" title="Coelurosauria">coelurosaurs</a>, which are theropods, which are saurischians, which are dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research by Matthew G. Baron, <a href="/wiki/David_B._Norman" title="David B. Norman">David B. Norman</a>, and Paul M. Barrett in 2017 suggested a radical revision of dinosaurian systematics. Phylogenetic analysis by Baron <i>et al.</i> recovered the Ornithischia as being closer to the Theropoda than the Sauropodomorpha, as opposed to the traditional union of theropods with sauropodomorphs. They resurrected the clade <a href="/wiki/Ornithoscelida" title="Ornithoscelida">Ornithoscelida</a> to refer to the group containing Ornithischia and Theropoda. Dinosauria itself was re-defined as the last common ancestor of <i>Triceratops horridus</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">Passer domesticus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Diplodocus" title="Diplodocus">Diplodocus carnegii</a></i>, and all of its descendants, to ensure that sauropods and kin remain included as dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170322_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170322-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20170322_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20170322-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_description">General description</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3460" data-file-width="4560" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg/250px-LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg/375px-LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg/500px-LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops</a></i> skeleton, <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum_of_Los_Angeles_County" title="Natural History Museum of Los Angeles County">Natural History Museum of Los Angeles County</a></div></div></div>
<p>Using one of the above definitions, dinosaurs can be generally described as <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurs</a> with <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_locomotion#Posture" title="Terrestrial locomotion">hind limbs held erect beneath the body</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFG97_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-DFG97-15">[14]</a></sup> Other prehistoric animals, including <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosasaur" title="Mosasaur">mosasaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ichthyosaur" title="Ichthyosaur">ichthyosaurs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plesiosauria" title="Plesiosauria">plesiosaurs</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Dimetrodon" title="Dimetrodon">Dimetrodon</a></i>, while often popularly conceived of as dinosaurs, are not taxonomically classified as dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-16">[15]</a></sup> Pterosaurs are distantly related to dinosaurs, being members of the clade <a href="/wiki/Avemetatarsalia" title="Avemetatarsalia">Ornithodira</a>. The other groups mentioned are, like dinosaurs and pterosaurs, members of <a href="/wiki/Sauropsida" title="Sauropsida">Sauropsida</a> (the reptile and bird clade), except <i>Dimetrodon</i> (which is a <a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">synapsid</a>). None of them had the erect hind limb posture characteristic of true dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DL90_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-DL90-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dinosaurs were the dominant terrestrial vertebrates of the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> <a href="/wiki/Era" title="Era">Era</a>, especially the Jurassic and Cretaceous periods. Other groups of animals were restricted in size and niches; <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, for example, rarely exceeded the size of a domestic cat, and were generally rodent-sized carnivores of small prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MM97_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-MM97-18">[17]</a></sup> They have always been recognized as an extremely varied group of animals; over 900 non-avian dinosaur genera have been identified with certainty as of 2018, and the total number of genera preserved in the fossil record has been estimated at around 1850, nearly 75% of which remain to be discovered, and 1124 species by 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genera900_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genera900-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howmany_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howmany-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang&amp;Dodson_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang&amp;Dodson-21">[20]</a></sup> A 1995 study predicted that about 3,400 dinosaur genera ever existed, including many that would not have been preserved in the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell1995_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell1995-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, the estimated number of dinosaur species that existed in the Mesozoic was 1,543–2,468.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> In 2021, the number of modern-day birds (avian dinosaurs) was estimated to be at 10,806 species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> Some are herbivorous, others carnivorous, including seed-eaters, fish-eaters, insectivores, and omnivores. While dinosaurs were ancestrally bipedal (as are all modern birds), some prehistoric species were quadrupeds, and others, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Anchisaurus" title="Anchisaurus">Anchisaurus</a></i> and <i>Iguanodon</i>, could walk just as easily on two or four legs. Cranial modifications like horns and crests are common dinosaurian traits, and some extinct species had bony armor. Although known for large size, many Mesozoic dinosaurs were human-sized or smaller, and modern birds are generally small in size. Dinosaurs today inhabit every continent, and fossils show that they had achieved global distribution by at least the <a href="/wiki/Early_Jurassic" title="Early Jurassic">Early Jurassic</a> epoch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacLeod_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod-26">[25]</a></sup> Modern birds inhabit most available habitats, from terrestrial to marine, and there is evidence that some non-avian dinosaurs (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Microraptor" title="Microraptor">Microraptor</a></i>) could fly or at least glide, and others, such as <a href="/wiki/Spinosauridae" title="Spinosauridae">spinosaurids</a>, had <a href="/wiki/Semiaquatic" title="Semiaquatic">semiaquatic</a> habits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theropods_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-theropods-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distinguishing_anatomical_features">Distinguishing anatomical features</span></h3>
<p>While recent discoveries have made it more difficult to present a universally agreed-upon list of their distinguishing features, nearly all dinosaurs discovered so far share certain modifications to the ancestral archosaurian skeleton, or are clearly descendants of older dinosaurs showing these modifications. Although some later groups of dinosaurs featured further modified versions of these traits, they are considered typical for Dinosauria; the earliest dinosaurs had them and passed them on to their descendants. Such modifications, originating in the most recent common ancestor of a certain taxonomic group, are called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synapomorphy_and_apomorphy" title="Synapomorphy and apomorphy">synapomorphies</a> of such a group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B012_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-B012-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dromaeosaurus_skull_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="680" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dromaeosaurus_skull_en.svg/220px-Dromaeosaurus_skull_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dromaeosaurus_skull_en.svg/330px-Dromaeosaurus_skull_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dromaeosaurus_skull_en.svg/440px-Dromaeosaurus_skull_en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dromaeosaurus_skull_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Labeled diagram of a typical archosaur skull, the skull of <i><a href="/wiki/Dromaeosaurus" title="Dromaeosaurus">Dromaeosaurus</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>A detailed assessment of archosaur interrelations by <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Nesbitt" title="Sterling Nesbitt">Sterling Nesbitt</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nesbitt2011_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-nesbitt2011-29">[28]</a></sup> confirmed or found the following twelve unambiguous synapomorphies, some previously known:
</p>
<ul><li>In the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a>, a supratemporal fossa (excavation) is present in front of the <a href="/wiki/Skull#Structure" title="Skull">supratemporal fenestra</a>, the main opening in the rear skull roof</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epipophyses" title="Epipophyses">Epipophyses</a>, obliquely backward-pointing processes on the rear top corners of the anterior (front) neck <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">vertebrae</a> behind the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(anatomy)" title="Atlas (anatomy)">atlas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Axis_(anatomy)" title="Axis (anatomy)">axis</a>, the first two neck vertebrae</li>
<li>Apex of a deltopectoral crest (a projection on which the <a href="/wiki/Clavipectoral_triangle" title="Clavipectoral triangle">deltopectoral</a> muscles attach) located at or more than 30% down the length of the <a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">humerus</a> (upper arm bone)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Radius_(bone)" title="Radius (bone)">Radius</a>, a lower arm bone, shorter than 80% of humerus length</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fourth_trochanter" title="Fourth trochanter">Fourth trochanter</a> (projection where the <a href="/wiki/Caudofemoralis" title="Caudofemoralis">caudofemoralis</a> muscle attaches on the inner rear shaft) on the <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a> (thigh bone) is a sharp flange</li>
<li>Fourth trochanter asymmetrical, with distal, lower, margin forming a steeper angle to the shaft</li>
<li>On the <a href="/wiki/Talus_bone" title="Talus bone">astragalus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcaneus" title="Calcaneus">calcaneum</a>, upper ankle bones, the proximal articular facet, the top connecting surface, for the <a href="/wiki/Fibula" title="Fibula">fibula</a> occupies less than 30% of the transverse width of the element</li>
<li>Exoccipitals (bones at the back of the skull) do not meet along the midline on the floor of the endocranial cavity, the inner space of the braincase</li>
<li>In the pelvis, the proximal articular surfaces of the <a href="/wiki/Ischium" title="Ischium">ischium</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">ilium</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubis</a> are separated by a large concave surface (on the upper side of the ischium a part of the open hip joint is located between the contacts with the pubic bone and the ilium)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cnemial_crest" title="Cnemial crest">Cnemial crest</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">tibia</a> (protruding part of the top surface of the shinbone) arcs anterolaterally (curves to the front and the outer side)</li>
<li>Distinct proximodistally oriented (vertical) ridge present on the posterior face of the distal end of the tibia (the rear surface of the lower end of the shinbone)</li>
<li>Concave articular surface for the fibula of the calcaneum (the top surface of the calcaneum, where it touches the fibula, has a hollow profile)</li></ul>
<p>Nesbitt found a number of further potential synapomorphies and discounted a number of synapomorphies previously suggested. Some of these are also present in <a href="/wiki/Silesauridae" title="Silesauridae">silesaurids</a>, which Nesbitt recovered as a sister group to Dinosauria, including a large anterior trochanter, metatarsals II and IV of subequal length, reduced contact between ischium and pubis, the presence of a cnemial crest on the tibia and of an ascending process on the astragalus, and many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB04dino_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-MJB04dino-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="492" decoding="async" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg/220px-Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg/330px-Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg/440px-Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hip joints and hindlimb postures of: (left to right) typical <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> (sprawling), dinosaurs and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> (erect), and <a href="/wiki/Rauisuchia" title="Rauisuchia">rauisuchians</a> (pillar-erect)</div></div></div>
<p>A variety of other skeletal features are shared by dinosaurs. However, because they are either common to other groups of archosaurs or were not present in all early dinosaurs, these features are not considered to be synapomorphies. For example, as <a href="/wiki/Diapsid" title="Diapsid">diapsids</a>, dinosaurs ancestrally had two pairs of <a href="/wiki/Infratemporal_fenestra" title="Infratemporal fenestra">Infratemporal fenestrae</a> (openings in the skull behind the eyes), and as members of the diapsid group Archosauria, had additional openings in the <a href="/wiki/Antorbital_fenestra" title="Antorbital fenestra">snout</a> and lower jaw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TRHJ00_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-TRHJ00-30">[29]</a></sup> Additionally, several characteristics once thought to be synapomorphies are now known to have appeared before dinosaurs, or were absent in the earliest dinosaurs and independently evolved by different dinosaur groups. These include an elongated <a href="/wiki/Scapula" title="Scapula">scapula</a>, or shoulder blade; a <a href="/wiki/Sacrum" title="Sacrum">sacrum</a> composed of three or more fused vertebrae (three are found in some other archosaurs, but only two are found in <i><a href="/wiki/Herrerasaurus" title="Herrerasaurus">Herrerasaurus</a></i>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB04dino_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-MJB04dino-8">[7]</a></sup> and a perforate <a href="/wiki/Acetabulum" title="Acetabulum">acetabulum</a>, or hip socket, with a hole at the center of its inside surface (closed in <i><a href="/wiki/Saturnalia_tupiniquim" title="Saturnalia tupiniquim">Saturnalia tupiniquim</a></i>, for example).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UC_Berkeley_Journal_of_Earth_Sciences_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-UC_Berkeley_Journal_of_Earth_Sciences-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LARB99_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-LARB99-32">[31]</a></sup> Another difficulty of determining distinctly dinosaurian features is that early dinosaurs and other archosaurs from the <a href="/wiki/Late_Triassic" title="Late Triassic">Late Triassic</a> epoch are often poorly known and were similar in many ways; these animals have sometimes been misidentified in the literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIP07_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIP07-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dinosaurs stand with their hind limbs erect in a manner similar to <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_mammals#Erect_limbs" title="Evolution of mammals">most modern mammals</a>, but distinct from most other reptiles, whose limbs sprawl out to either side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland1909_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holland1909-34">[33]</a></sup> This posture is due to the development of a laterally facing recess in the pelvis (usually an open socket) and a corresponding inwardly facing distinct head on the femur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB00_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-MJB00-35">[34]</a></sup> Their erect posture enabled early dinosaurs to breathe easily while moving, which likely permitted stamina and activity levels that <a href="/wiki/Carrier%27s_constraint" title="Carrier's constraint">surpassed those of "sprawling" reptiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RC05_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-RC05-36">[35]</a></sup> Erect limbs probably also helped support the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> of large size by reducing bending stresses on limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TKMB07_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-TKMB07-37">[36]</a></sup> Some non-dinosaurian archosaurs, including <a href="/wiki/Rauisuchia" title="Rauisuchia">rauisuchians</a>, also had erect limbs but achieved this by a "pillar-erect" configuration of the hip joint, where instead of having a projection from the femur insert on a socket on the hip, the <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">upper pelvic bone</a> was rotated to form an overhanging shelf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TKMB07_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-TKMB07-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_study">History of study</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_paleontology" title="History of paleontology">History of paleontology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-scientific_history">Pre-scientific history</span></h3>
<p>Dinosaur fossils have been known for millennia, although their true nature was not recognized. The Chinese considered them to be <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">dragon</a> bones and documented them as such. For example, <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicles_of_Huayang" title="Chronicles of Huayang">Huayang Guo Zhi</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">華陽國志</span>), a <a href="/wiki/Gazetteer" title="Gazetteer">gazetteer</a> compiled by <a href="/wiki/Chang_Qu" title="Chang Qu">Chang Qu</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">常璩</span>) during the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(266%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (266–420)">Western Jin Dynasty</a> (265–316), reported the discovery of dragon bones at Wucheng in <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> Province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dong1992_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-dong1992-38">[37]</a></sup> Villagers in central <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> have long unearthed fossilized "dragon bones" for use in <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional medicines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCdinobonemed_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCdinobonemed-39">[38]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, dinosaur fossils were generally believed to be the remains of <a href="/wiki/Giant" title="Giant">giants</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">biblical</a> creatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benton2000_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-benton2000-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_dinosaur_research">Early dinosaur research</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Buckland_detail.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="130" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2b/William_Buckland_detail.png" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Buckland_detail.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Buckland" title="William Buckland">William Buckland</a></div></div></div>
<p>Scholarly descriptions of what would now be recognized as dinosaur bones first appeared in the late 17th century in England. Part of a bone, now known to have been the femur of a <i>Megalosaurus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAS97_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-WAS97-41">[40]</a></sup> was recovered from a limestone quarry at <a href="/wiki/Cornwell,_Oxfordshire" title="Cornwell, Oxfordshire">Cornwell</a> near <a href="/wiki/Chipping_Norton" title="Chipping Norton">Chipping Norton</a>, Oxfordshire, in 1676. The fragment was sent to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Plot" title="Robert Plot">Robert Plot</a>, Professor of Chemistry at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> and first curator of the <a href="/wiki/Ashmolean_Museum" title="Ashmolean Museum">Ashmolean Museum</a>, who published a description in his <i>The Natural History of Oxford-shire</i> (1677).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> He correctly identified the bone as the lower extremity of the femur of a large animal, and recognized that it was too large to belong to any known species. He, therefore, concluded it to be the femur of a huge human, perhaps a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titan</a> or another type of giant featured in legends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lhuyd" title="Edward Lhuyd">Edward Lhuyd</a>, a friend of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a>, published <i>Lithophylacii Britannici ichnographia</i> (1699), the first scientific treatment of what would now be recognized as a dinosaur when he described and named a sauropod <a href="/wiki/Tooth" title="Tooth">tooth</a>, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rutellum" title="Rutellum">Rutellum impicatum</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L99_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-L99-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DS02_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-DS02-46">[45]</a></sup> that had been found in Caswell, near <a href="/wiki/Witney" title="Witney">Witney</a>, Oxfordshire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G45_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-G45-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dinosaur_coining_of_the_word_in_1841.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1571" data-file-width="1687" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Dinosaur_coining_of_the_word_in_1841.jpg/220px-Dinosaur_coining_of_the_word_in_1841.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Dinosaur_coining_of_the_word_in_1841.jpg/330px-Dinosaur_coining_of_the_word_in_1841.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Dinosaur_coining_of_the_word_in_1841.jpg/440px-Dinosaur_coining_of_the_word_in_1841.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dinosaur_coining_of_the_word_in_1841.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Sir Richard Owen's</a> coining of the word <i>dinosaur</i>, at a meeting of the <a href="/wiki/British_Science_Association" title="British Science Association">British Association for the Advancement of Science</a> in 1841</div></div></div>
<p>Between 1815 and 1824, the Rev <a href="/wiki/William_Buckland" title="William Buckland">William Buckland</a>, the first Reader of Geology at the University of Oxford, collected more fossilized bones of <i>Megalosaurus</i> and became the first person to describe a non-avian dinosaur in a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_journal" title="Scientific journal">scientific journal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAS97_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-WAS97-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buckland1824_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-buckland1824-48">[47]</a></sup> The second non-avian dinosaur genus to be identified, <i>Iguanodon</i>, was discovered in 1822 by <a href="/wiki/Mary_Ann_Mantell" title="Mary Ann Mantell">Mary Ann Mantell</a> – the wife of English geologist <a href="/wiki/Gideon_Mantell" title="Gideon Mantell">Gideon Mantell</a>. Gideon Mantell recognized similarities between his fossils and the bones of modern <a href="/wiki/Iguana" title="Iguana">iguanas</a>. He published his findings in 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GM25_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-GM25-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDS97_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-HDS97-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The study of these "great fossil lizards" soon became of great interest to European and American scientists, and in 1841 the English paleontologist Sir Richard Owen coined the term "dinosaur", using it to refer to the "distinct tribe or sub-order of Saurian Reptiles" that were then being recognized in England and around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen1841_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owen1841-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> The term is derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B4%CE%B5%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C%CF%82" title="wikt:δεινός">δεινός</a><i> (deinos)</i> 'terrible, potent or fearfully great', and <i> </i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%83%CE%B1%E1%BF%A6%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:σαῦρος">σαῦρος</a><i> (sauros)</i> 'lizard or reptile'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen1841_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Owen1841-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSJ_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSJ-53">[52]</a></sup> Though the taxonomic name has often been interpreted as a reference to dinosaurs' teeth, claws, and other fearsome characteristics, Owen intended it to also evoke their size and majesty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FBS97_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FBS97-54">[53]</a></sup> Owen recognized that the remains that had been found so far, <i>Iguanodon</i>, <i>Megalosaurus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hylaeosaurus" title="Hylaeosaurus">Hylaeosaurus</a></i>, shared a number of distinctive features, and so decided to present them as a distinct taxonomic group. With the backing of <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Prince_Consort" title="Albert, Prince Consort">Prince Albert</a>, the husband of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>, Owen established the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_London" title="Natural History Museum, London">Natural History Museum, London</a>, to display the national collection of dinosaur fossils and other biological and geological exhibits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-owen94_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-owen94-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discoveries_in_North_America">Discoveries in North America</span></h3>
<p>In 1858, <a href="/wiki/William_Parker_Foulke" title="William Parker Foulke">William Parker Foulke</a> discovered the first known American dinosaur, in <a href="/wiki/Marl" title="Marl">marl</a> pits in the small town of <a href="/wiki/Haddonfield,_New_Jersey" title="Haddonfield, New Jersey">Haddonfield, New Jersey</a>. (Although fossils had been found before, their nature had not been correctly discerned.) The creature was named <i><a href="/wiki/Hadrosaurus" title="Hadrosaurus">Hadrosaurus foulkii</a></i>. It was an extremely important find: <i>Hadrosaurus</i> was one of the first nearly complete dinosaur skeletons found (<a href="/wiki/Iguanodon#Gideon_Mantell,_Sir_Richard_Owen,_and_the_discovery_of_dinosaurs" title="Iguanodon">the first</a> was in 1834, in <a href="/wiki/Maidstone" title="Maidstone">Maidstone, England</a>), and it was clearly a bipedal creature. This was a revolutionary discovery as, until that point, most scientists had believed dinosaurs walked on four feet, like other lizards. Foulke's discoveries sparked a wave of interests in dinosaurs in the United States, known as dinosaur mania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weishampel06_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-weishampel06-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p>

//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/OthnielCharlesMarsh.jpg/296px-OthnielCharlesMarsh.jpg 2x" width="148"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center"><a href="/wiki/Othniel_Charles_Marsh" title="Othniel Charles Marsh">Othniel Charles Marsh</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Dinosaur mania was exemplified by the fierce rivalry between <a href="/wiki/Edward_Drinker_Cope" title="Edward Drinker Cope">Edward Drinker Cope</a> and <a href="/wiki/Othniel_Charles_Marsh" title="Othniel Charles Marsh">Othniel Charles Marsh</a>, both of whom raced to be the first to find new dinosaurs in what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Bone_Wars" title="Bone Wars">Bone Wars</a>. This fight between the two scientists lasted for over 30 years, ending in 1897 when Cope died after spending his entire fortune on the dinosaur hunt. Unfortunately, many valuable dinosaur specimens were damaged or destroyed due to the pair's rough methods: for example, their diggers often used <a href="/wiki/Dynamite" title="Dynamite">dynamite</a> to unearth bones. Modern paleontologists would find such methods crude and unacceptable, since blasting easily destroys fossil and stratigraphic evidence. Despite their unrefined methods, the contributions of Cope and Marsh to paleontology were vast: Marsh unearthed 86 new species of dinosaur and Cope discovered 56, a total of 142 new species. Cope's collection is now at the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a> in New York City, while Marsh's is at the <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Peabody Museum of Natural History">Peabody Museum of Natural History</a> at <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".22Dinosaur_renaissance.22_and_beyond"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Dinosaur_renaissance"_and_beyond'>"Dinosaur renaissance" and beyond</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_renaissance" title="Dinosaur renaissance">Dinosaur renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="907" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg/220px-Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg/330px-Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg/440px-Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paleontologist <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._Bakker" title="Robert T. Bakker">Robert T. Bakker</a> with mounted skeleton of a <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">tyrannosaurid</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Gorgosaurus" title="Gorgosaurus">Gorgosaurus libratus</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>The field of dinosaur research has enjoyed a surge in activity that began in the 1970s and is ongoing. This was triggered, in part, by <a href="/wiki/John_Ostrom" title="John Ostrom">John Ostrom</a>'s discovery and 1969 description of <i><a href="/wiki/Deinonychus" title="Deinonychus">Deinonychus</a></i>, an active predator that may have been warm-blooded, in marked contrast to the then-prevailing image of dinosaurs as sluggish and cold-blooded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker86_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-bakker86-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker1968_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-bakker1968-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker1972_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-bakker1972-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Results_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Results-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor2010_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor2010-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naish_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naish-63">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate_paleontology" title="Vertebrate paleontology">Vertebrate paleontology</a> has become a global <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>. Major new dinosaur discoveries have been made by paleontologists working in previously unexploited regions, including India, South America, Madagascar, Antarctica, and most significantly China (the well-preserved feathered dinosaurs in China have further consolidated the link between dinosaurs and their living descendants, modern birds). The widespread application of <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistics</a>, which rigorously analyzes the relationships between biological organisms, has also proved tremendously useful in classifying dinosaurs. Cladistic analysis, among other modern techniques, helps to compensate for an often incomplete and fragmentary fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161208_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161208-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soft_tissue_and_DNA">Soft tissue and DNA</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:9121_-_Milano,_Museo_storia_naturale_-_Scipionyx_samniticus_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto_22-Apr-2007a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1360" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/9121_-_Milano%2C_Museo_storia_naturale_-_Scipionyx_samniticus_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto_22-Apr-2007a.jpg/220px-9121_-_Milano%2C_Museo_storia_naturale_-_Scipionyx_samniticus_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto_22-Apr-2007a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/9121_-_Milano%2C_Museo_storia_naturale_-_Scipionyx_samniticus_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto_22-Apr-2007a.jpg/330px-9121_-_Milano%2C_Museo_storia_naturale_-_Scipionyx_samniticus_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto_22-Apr-2007a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/9121_-_Milano%2C_Museo_storia_naturale_-_Scipionyx_samniticus_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto_22-Apr-2007a.jpg/440px-9121_-_Milano%2C_Museo_storia_naturale_-_Scipionyx_samniticus_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto_22-Apr-2007a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:9121_-_Milano,_Museo_storia_naturale_-_Scipionyx_samniticus_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto_22-Apr-2007a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Scipionyx" title="Scipionyx">Scipionyx</a></i> fossil with intestines, <a href="/wiki/Museo_Civico_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Milano" title="Museo Civico di Storia Naturale di Milano">Natural History Museum of Milan</a></div></div></div>
<p>One of the best examples of soft-tissue impressions in a fossil dinosaur was discovered in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pietraroja_Formation" title="Pietraroja Formation">Pietraroja Plattenkalk</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. The discovery was reported in 1998, and described the specimen of a small, juvenile coelurosaur, <i><a href="/wiki/Scipionyx" title="Scipionyx">Scipionyx samniticus</a></i>. The fossil includes portions of the intestines, colon, liver, muscles, and windpipe of this dinosaur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-softtissue_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-softtissue-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the March 2005 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, the paleontologist <a href="/wiki/Mary_Higby_Schweitzer" title="Mary Higby Schweitzer">Mary Higby Schweitzer</a> and her team announced the discovery of flexible material resembling actual soft tissue inside a 68-million-year-old <i>Tyrannosaurus rex</i> leg bone from the <a href="/wiki/Hell_Creek_Formation" title="Hell Creek Formation">Hell Creek Formation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>. After recovery, the tissue was rehydrated by the science team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzer2005_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer2005-66">[65]</a></sup> When the fossilized bone was treated over several weeks to remove mineral content from the fossilized bone-marrow cavity (a process called demineralization), Schweitzer found evidence of intact structures such as <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteon" title="Osteon">bone matrix</a>, and connective tissue (bone fibers). Scrutiny under the microscope further revealed that the putative dinosaur soft tissue had retained fine structures (microstructures) even at the cellular level. The exact nature and composition of this material, and the implications of Schweitzer's discovery, are not yet clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzer2005_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer2005-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, a team including Schweitzer announced that, using even more careful methodology, they had duplicated their results by finding similar soft tissue in a duck-billed dinosaur, <i><a href="/wiki/Brachylophosaurus" title="Brachylophosaurus">Brachylophosaurus canadensis</a></i>, found in the <a href="/wiki/Judith_River_Formation" title="Judith River Formation">Judith River Formation</a> of Montana. This included even more detailed tissue, down to preserved bone cells that seem to have visible remnants of nuclei and what seem to be red blood cells. Among other materials found in the bone was <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>, as in the <i>Tyrannosaurus</i> bone. The type of collagen an animal has in its bones varies according to its <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and, in both cases, this collagen was of the same type found in modern chickens and ostriches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The extraction of ancient DNA from dinosaur fossils has been reported on two separate occasions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bone_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-bone-68">[67]</a></sup> upon further inspection and <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer review</a>, however, neither of these reports could be confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wangetal1997_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wangetal1997-69">[68]</a></sup> However, a functional <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptide</a> involved in the vision of a theoretical dinosaur has been inferred using analytical phylogenetic reconstruction methods on gene sequences of related modern species such as reptiles and birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-changetal2002_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-changetal2002-70">[69]</a></sup> In addition, several <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzeretal1997_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzeretal1997-71">[70]</a></sup> have putatively been detected in dinosaur fossils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emberyatal2003_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emberyatal2003-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, researchers reported finding structures similar to blood cells and collagen fibers, preserved in the bone fossils of six Cretaceous dinosaur specimens, which are approximately 75 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_early_evolution">Origins and early evolution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg"><img alt="Full skeleton of an early carnivorous dinosaur, displayed in a glass case in a museum" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1604" data-file-width="2549" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg/220px-Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg/330px-Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg/440px-Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The early dinosaurs <i><a href="/wiki/Herrerasaurus" title="Herrerasaurus">Herrerasaurus</a></i> (large), <i><a href="/wiki/Eoraptor" title="Eoraptor">Eoraptor</a></i> (small) and a <i><a href="/wiki/Plateosaurus" title="Plateosaurus">Plateosaurus</a></i> skull, from the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a></div></div></div>
<p>Dinosaurs diverged from their archosaur ancestors during the Middle to Late Triassic epochs, roughly 20 million years after the devastating <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a> wiped out an estimated 96% of all marine species and 70% of terrestrial vertebrate species approximately 252 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KPA_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-KPA-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TannerLucas_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-TannerLucas-77">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">Radiometric dating</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ischigualasto_Formation" title="Ischigualasto Formation">Ischigualasto Formation</a> of Argentina where the early dinosaur <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Eoraptor" title="Eoraptor">Eoraptor</a></i> was found date it as 231.4 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OARM2010_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-OARM2010-78">[77]</a></sup> <i>Eoraptor</i> is thought to resemble the <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common ancestor</a> of all dinosaurs; if this is true, its traits suggest that the first dinosaurs were small, bipedal <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daemonosaurus_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daemonosaurus-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sereno1999_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sereno1999-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFRM93_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFRM93-81">[80]</a></sup> The discovery of primitive, dinosaur-like ornithodirans such as <i><a href="/wiki/Lagosuchus" title="Lagosuchus">Lagosuchus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Lagerpeton" title="Lagerpeton">Lagerpeton</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Carnian" title="Carnian">Carnian</a> epoch of the Triassic, around 233 million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mariscano2016_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-mariscano2016-82">[81]</a></sup> supports this view; analysis of recovered fossils suggests that these animals were indeed small, bipedal predators. Dinosaurs may have appeared as early as the <a href="/wiki/Anisian" title="Anisian">Anisian</a> epoch of the Triassic, 245 million years ago, as evidenced by remains of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Nyasasaurus" title="Nyasasaurus">Nyasasaurus</a></i> from that period. However, its known fossils are too fragmentary to tell if it was a dinosaur or only a close relative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyasasaurus_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyasasaurus-83">[82]</a></sup> Paleontologist Max C. Langer <i>et al.</i> (2018) determined that <i><a href="/wiki/Staurikosaurus" title="Staurikosaurus">Staurikosaurus</a></i> from the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_Formation" title="Santa Maria Formation">Santa Maria Formation</a> dates to 233.23 million years ago, making it older in geologic age than <i>Eoraptor</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langer18_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-langer18-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>When dinosaurs appeared, they were not the dominant terrestrial animals. The terrestrial habitats were occupied by various types of <a href="/wiki/Archosauromorpha" title="Archosauromorpha">archosauromorphs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Therapsid" title="Therapsid">therapsids</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Cynodont" title="Cynodont">cynodonts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhynchosaur" title="Rhynchosaur">rhynchosaurs</a>. Their main competitors were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudosuchians" title="Pseudosuchians">pseudosuchians</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Aetosaur" title="Aetosaur">aetosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ornithosuchidae" title="Ornithosuchidae">ornithosuchids</a> and rauisuchians, which were more successful than the dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Most of these other animals became extinct in the Triassic, in one of two events. First, at about 215 million years ago, a variety of <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> archosauromorphs, including the <a href="/wiki/Protorosauria" title="Protorosauria">protorosaurs</a>, became extinct. This was followed by the Triassic–Jurassic extinction event (about 201 million years ago), that saw the end of most of the other groups of early archosaurs, like aetosaurs, ornithosuchids, <a href="/wiki/Phytosaur" title="Phytosaur">phytosaurs</a>, and rauisuchians. Rhynchosaurs and <a href="/wiki/Dicynodont" title="Dicynodont">dicynodonts</a> survived (at least in some areas) at least as late as early –mid <a href="/wiki/Norian" title="Norian">Norian</a> and late Norian or earliest <a href="/wiki/Rhaetian" title="Rhaetian">Rhaetian</a> <a href="/wiki/Stage_(stratigraphy)" title="Stage (stratigraphy)">stages</a>, respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> and the exact date of their <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> is uncertain. These losses left behind a land fauna of <a href="/wiki/Crocodylomorpha" title="Crocodylomorpha">crocodylomorphs</a>, dinosaurs, mammals, pterosaurians, and <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB04dino_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-MJB04dino-8">[7]</a></sup> The first few lines of early dinosaurs <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">diversified</a> through the Carnian and Norian stages of the Triassic, possibly by occupying the niches of the groups that became extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letal05_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Letal05-10">[9]</a></sup> Also notably, there was a heightened rate of extinction during the <a href="/wiki/Carnian_pluvial_episode" title="Carnian pluvial episode">Carnian pluvial event</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_and_paleobiogeography">Evolution and paleobiogeography</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pangaea_200Ma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="759" data-file-width="1519" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pangaea_200Ma.jpg/330px-Pangaea_200Ma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pangaea_200Ma.jpg/495px-Pangaea_200Ma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pangaea_200Ma.jpg/660px-Pangaea_200Ma.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pangaea_200Ma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> in the early <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> (around 200 million years ago)</div></div></div>
<p>Dinosaur evolution after the Triassic followed changes in vegetation and the location of continents. In the Late Triassic and Early Jurassic, the continents were connected as the single landmass <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>, and there was a worldwide dinosaur fauna mostly composed of <a href="/wiki/Coelophysoidea" title="Coelophysoidea">coelophysoid</a> carnivores and early sauropodomorph herbivores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCL04_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-HCL04-89">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">Gymnosperm</a> plants (particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">conifers</a>), a potential food source, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_radiation" title="Evolutionary radiation">radiated</a> in the Late Triassic. Early sauropodomorphs did not have sophisticated mechanisms for processing food in the mouth, and so must have employed other means of breaking down food farther along the digestive tract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-90">[89]</a></sup> The general homogeneity of dinosaurian faunas continued into the Middle and Late Jurassic, where most localities had predators consisting of <a href="/wiki/Ceratosauria" title="Ceratosauria">ceratosaurians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megalosauroidea" title="Megalosauroidea">megalosauroids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Allosauroidea" title="Allosauroidea">allosauroids</a>, and herbivores consisting of stegosaurian ornithischians and large sauropods. Examples of this include the <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Formation" title="Morrison Formation">Morrison Formation</a> of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tendaguru_Formation" title="Tendaguru Formation">Tendaguru Beds</a> of Tanzania. Dinosaurs in China show some differences, with specialized <a href="/wiki/Metriacanthosauridae" title="Metriacanthosauridae">metriacanthosaurid</a> theropods and unusual, long-necked sauropods like <i><a href="/wiki/Mamenchisaurus" title="Mamenchisaurus">Mamenchisaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCL04_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-HCL04-89">[88]</a></sup> Ankylosaurians and ornithopods were also becoming more common, but primitive sauropodomorphs had become extinct. Conifers and <a href="/wiki/Pteridophyte" title="Pteridophyte">pteridophytes</a> were the most common plants. Sauropods, like earlier sauropodomorphs, were not oral processors, but ornithischians were evolving various means of dealing with food in the mouth, including potential <a href="/wiki/Cheek" title="Cheek">cheek</a>-like organs to keep food in the mouth, and jaw motions to grind food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-90">[89]</a></sup> Another notable evolutionary event of the Jurassic was the appearance of true birds, descended from maniraptoran coelurosaurians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the <a href="/wiki/Early_Cretaceous" title="Early Cretaceous">Early Cretaceous</a> and the ongoing breakup of Pangaea, dinosaurs were becoming strongly differentiated by landmass. The earliest part of this time saw the spread of ankylosaurians, <a href="/wiki/Iguanodontia" title="Iguanodontia">iguanodontians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brachiosauridae" title="Brachiosauridae">brachiosaurids</a> through Europe, North America, and northern <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. These were later supplemented or replaced in Africa by large spinosaurid and <a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosauridae" title="Carcharodontosauridae">carcharodontosaurid</a> theropods, and <a href="/wiki/Rebbachisauridae" title="Rebbachisauridae">rebbachisaurid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titanosauria" title="Titanosauria">titanosaurian</a> sauropods, also found in <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, maniraptoran coelurosaurians like <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">dromaeosaurids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Troodontidae" title="Troodontidae">troodontids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oviraptorosauria" title="Oviraptorosauria">oviraptorosaurians</a> became the common theropods, and <a href="/wiki/Ankylosauridae" title="Ankylosauridae">ankylosaurids</a> and early ceratopsians like <i>Psittacosaurus</i> became important herbivores. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> was home to a fauna of basal ankylosaurians, <a href="/wiki/Hypsilophodont" title="Hypsilophodont">hypsilophodonts</a>, and iguanodontians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCL04_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-HCL04-89">[88]</a></sup> The stegosaurians appear to have gone extinct at some point in the late Early Cretaceous or early <a href="/wiki/Late_Cretaceous" title="Late Cretaceous">Late Cretaceous</a>. A major change in the Early Cretaceous, which would be amplified in the Late Cretaceous, was the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>. At the same time, several groups of dinosaurian herbivores evolved more sophisticated ways to orally process food. Ceratopsians developed a method of slicing with teeth stacked on each other in batteries, and iguanodontians refined a method of grinding with <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_tooth#Dental_batteries" title="Dinosaur tooth">dental batteries</a>, taken to its extreme in hadrosaurids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-90">[89]</a></sup> Some sauropods also evolved tooth batteries, best exemplified by the rebbachisaurid <i><a href="/wiki/Nigersaurus" title="Nigersaurus">Nigersaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serenoetal07_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-serenoetal07-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were three general dinosaur faunas in the Late Cretaceous. In the northern continents of North America and Asia, the major theropods were <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">tyrannosaurids</a> and various types of smaller maniraptoran theropods, with a predominantly ornithischian herbivore assemblage of hadrosaurids, ceratopsians, ankylosaurids, and pachycephalosaurians. In the southern continents that had made up the now-splitting supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abelisauridae" title="Abelisauridae">abelisaurids</a> were the common theropods, and titanosaurian sauropods the common herbivores. Finally, in Europe, dromaeosaurids, <a href="/wiki/Rhabdodontidae" title="Rhabdodontidae">rhabdodontid</a> iguanodontians, <a href="/wiki/Nodosauridae" title="Nodosauridae">nodosaurid</a> ankylosaurians, and titanosaurian sauropods were prevalent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCL04_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-HCL04-89">[88]</a></sup> Flowering plants were greatly radiating,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-90">[89]</a></sup> with the first grasses appearing by the end of the Cretaceous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSAS05_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSAS05-92">[91]</a></sup> Grinding hadrosaurids and shearing ceratopsians became very diverse across North America and Asia. Theropods were also radiating as herbivores or <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Therizinosauria" title="Therizinosauria">therizinosaurians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ornithomimosauria" title="Ornithomimosauria">ornithomimosaurians</a> becoming common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_90-4"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event, which occurred approximately 66 million years ago at the end of the Cretaceous, caused the extinction of all dinosaur groups except for the neornithine birds. Some other diapsid groups, including <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodilians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dyrosauridae" title="Dyrosauridae">dyrosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sebecosuchia" title="Sebecosuchia">sebecosuchians</a>, turtles, <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhynchocephalia" title="Rhynchocephalia">sphenodontians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Choristodera" title="Choristodera">choristoderans</a>, also survived the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF04_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-AF04-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The surviving lineages of neornithine birds, including the ancestors of modern <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fowl" title="Fowl">ducks and chickens</a>, and a variety of <a href="/wiki/Charadriiformes" title="Charadriiformes">waterbirds</a>, diversified rapidly at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> period, entering <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niches</a> left vacant by the extinction of Mesozoic dinosaur groups such as the arboreal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enantiornithine" title="Enantiornithine">enantiornithines</a>, aquatic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hesperornithine" title="Hesperornithine">hesperornithines</a>, and even the larger terrestrial theropods (in the form of <i><a href="/wiki/Gastornis" title="Gastornis">Gastornis</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Eogruidae" title="Eogruidae">eogruiids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathornithids" title="Bathornithids">bathornithids</a>, ratites, <a href="/wiki/Geranoididae" title="Geranoididae">geranoidids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mihirung" title="Mihirung">mihirungs</a>, and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terror_bird" title="Terror bird">terror birds</a>"). It is often stated that mammals out-competed the neornithines for dominance of most terrestrial niches but many of these groups co-existed with rich mammalian faunas for most of the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> Era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindow_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-lindow-94">[93]</a></sup> Terror birds and bathornithids occupied carnivorous guilds alongside predatory mammals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cracraft_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cracraft-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> and ratites are still fairly successful as mid-sized herbivores; eogruiids similarly lasted from the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> to <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a>, only becoming extinct very recently after over 20 million years of co-existence with many mammal groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_classification" title="Dinosaur classification">Dinosaur classification</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:188px;max-width:188px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:173px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Saurischia_pelvis.png/372px-Saurischia_pelvis.png 2x" width="186"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Saurischia" title="Saurischia">Saurischian</a> pelvis structure (left side)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:150px;max-width:150px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:173px;overflow:hidden"><a
