href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i> pelvis (showing saurischian structure – left side)</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:148px;max-width:148px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:130px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ornithischia_pelvis.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="580" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Ornithischia_pelvis.png/146px-Ornithischia_pelvis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Ornithischia_pelvis.png/219px-Ornithischia_pelvis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Ornithischia_pelvis.png/292px-Ornithischia_pelvis.png 2x" width="146"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">Ornithischian</a> pelvis structure (left side)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:190px;max-width:190px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:130px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edmontosaurus_pelvis_left.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1784" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Edmontosaurus_pelvis_left.jpg/188px-Edmontosaurus_pelvis_left.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Edmontosaurus_pelvis_left.jpg/282px-Edmontosaurus_pelvis_left.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Edmontosaurus_pelvis_left.jpg/376px-Edmontosaurus_pelvis_left.jpg 2x" width="188"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i><a href="/wiki/Edmontosaurus" title="Edmontosaurus">Edmontosaurus</a></i> pelvis (showing ornithischian structure – left side)</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Dinosaurs belong to a group known as archosaurs, which also includes modern crocodilians. Within the archosaur group, dinosaurs are differentiated most noticeably by their gait. Dinosaur legs extend directly beneath the body, whereas the legs of lizards and crocodilians sprawl out to either side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B012_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-B012-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Collectively, dinosaurs as a clade are divided into two primary branches, Saurischia and Ornithischia. Saurischia includes those taxa sharing a more recent common ancestor with birds than with Ornithischia, while Ornithischia includes all <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a> sharing a more recent common ancestor with <i>Triceratops</i> than with Saurischia. Anatomically, these two groups can be distinguished most noticeably by their <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">pelvic</a> structure. Early saurischians—"lizard-hipped", from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <i><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">sauros</i></span></i> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">σαῦρος</span></span>) meaning "lizard" and <i><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">ischion</i></span></i> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἰσχίον</span></span>) meaning "hip joint"—retained the hip structure of their ancestors, with a pubis bone directed <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">cranially</a>, or forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB00_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-MJB00-35">[34]</a></sup> This basic form was modified by rotating the pubis backward to varying degrees in several groups (<i>Herrerasaurus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-98">[97]</a></sup> therizinosauroids,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clarketal2004_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-clarketal2004-99">[98]</a></sup> dromaeosaurids,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAPM04_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-MAPM04-100">[99]</a></sup> and birds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-12">[11]</a></sup>). Saurischia includes the theropods (exclusively bipedal and with a wide variety of diets) and sauropodomorphs (long-necked herbivores which include advanced, quadrupedal groups).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theropods_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-theropods-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sauropodomorphs_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-sauropodomorphs-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>By contrast, ornithischians—"bird-hipped", from the Greek <i>ornitheios</i> (ὀρνίθειος) meaning "of a bird" and <i>ischion</i> (ἰσχίον) meaning "hip joint"—had a pelvis that superficially resembled a bird's pelvis: the pubic bone was oriented caudally (rear-pointing). Unlike birds, the ornithischian pubis also usually had an additional forward-pointing process. Ornithischia includes a variety of species that were primarily herbivores.
</p><p>Despite the terms "bird hip" (Ornithischia) and "lizard hip" (Saurischia), birds are not part of Ornithischia. Birds instead belong to Saurischia, the “lizard-hipped” dinosaurs—birds evolved from earlier dinosaurs with "lizard hips".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B012_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-B012-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h3>
<p>The following is a simplified classification of dinosaur groups based on their evolutionary relationships, and those of the main dinosaur groups Theropoda, Sauropodomorpha and Ornithischia, compiled by Justin Tweet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> Further details and other hypotheses of classification may be found on individual articles.
</p>
<ul><li>Dinosauria</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ornithopods_jconway.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="660" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Ornithopods_jconway.jpg/220px-Ornithopods_jconway.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Ornithopods_jconway.jpg/330px-Ornithopods_jconway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Ornithopods_jconway.jpg/440px-Ornithopods_jconway.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ornithopods_jconway.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Restoration of six <a href="/wiki/Ornithopoda" title="Ornithopoda">ornithopods</a>; far left: <i><a href="/wiki/Camptosaurus" title="Camptosaurus">Camptosaurus</a></i>, left: <i><a href="/wiki/Iguanodon" title="Iguanodon">Iguanodon</a></i>, center background: <i><a href="/wiki/Shantungosaurus" title="Shantungosaurus">Shantungosaurus</a></i>, center foreground: <i><a href="/wiki/Dryosaurus" title="Dryosaurus">Dryosaurus</a></i>, right: <i><a href="/wiki/Corythosaurus" title="Corythosaurus">Corythosaurus</a></i>, far right (large) <i><a href="/wiki/Tenontosaurus" title="Tenontosaurus">Tenontosaurus</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">Ornithischia</a> ("bird-hipped"; diverse bipedal and quadrupedal herbivores)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Heterodontosauridae" title="Heterodontosauridae">Heterodontosauridae</a> (small herbivores/omnivores with prominent <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canine-like teeth</a>)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Genasauria" title="Genasauria">Genasauria</a> ("cheeked lizards")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Thyreophora" title="Thyreophora">Thyreophora</a> (armored dinosaurs; bipeds and quadrupeds)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurypoda" title="Eurypoda">Eurypoda</a> (heavy, quadrupedal thyreophorans)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Stegosauria" title="Stegosauria">Stegosauria</a> (spikes and plates as primary armor)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Huayangosauridae" title="Huayangosauridae">Huayangosauridae</a> (small stegosaurs with flank osteoderms and tail clubs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Stegosauridae" title="Stegosauridae">Stegosauridae</a> (large stegosaurs)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Ankylosauria" title="Ankylosauria">Ankylosauria</a> (<a href="/wiki/Scute" title="Scute">scutes</a> as primary armor)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Parankylosauria" title="Parankylosauria">Parankylosauria</a> (small, southern ankylosaurs with <a href="/wiki/Macuahuitl" title="Macuahuitl">macuahuitl</a>-like tails)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Nodosauridae" title="Nodosauridae">Nodosauridae</a> (mostly spiky, club-less ankylosaurs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Ankylosauridae" title="Ankylosauridae">Ankylosauridae</a> (characterized by flat scutes)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Ankylosaurinae" title="Ankylosaurinae">Ankylosaurinae</a> (club-tailed ankylosaurids)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Neornithischia" title="Neornithischia">Neornithischia</a> ("new ornithischians")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Cerapoda" title="Cerapoda">Cerapoda</a> ("horned feet")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Marginocephalia" title="Marginocephalia">Marginocephalia</a> (characterized by a cranial growth)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Pachycephalosauria" title="Pachycephalosauria">Pachycephalosauria</a> (bipeds with domed or knobby growth on skulls)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Ceratopsia" title="Ceratopsia">Ceratopsia</a> (bipeds and quadrupeds; many had neck frills and horns)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Chaoyangsauridae" title="Chaoyangsauridae">Chaoyangsauridae</a> (small, frill-less basal ceratopsians)</li>
<li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoceratopsia" title="Neoceratopsia">Neoceratopsia</a> ("new ceratopsians")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Leptoceratopsidae" title="Leptoceratopsidae">Leptoceratopsidae</a> (little to no frills, hornless, with robust jaws)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Protoceratopsidae" title="Protoceratopsidae">Protoceratopsidae</a> (basal ceratopsians with small frills and stubby horns)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceratopsoidea" title="Ceratopsoidea">Ceratopsoidea</a> (large-horned ceratopsians)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Ceratopsidae" title="Ceratopsidae">Ceratopsidae</a> (large, elaborately ornamented ceratopsians)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Chasmosaurinae" title="Chasmosaurinae">Chasmosaurinae</a> (ceratopsids with enlarged brow horns)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Centrosaurinae" title="Centrosaurinae">Centrosaurinae</a> (ceratopsids mostly characterized by frill and nasal ornamentation)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasutoceratopsini" title="Nasutoceratopsini">Nasutoceratopsini</a> (centrosaurines with enlarged nasal cavities)</li>
<li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centrosaurini" title="Centrosaurini">Centrosaurini</a> (centrosaurines with enlarged nasal horns)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Pachyrhinosaurini" title="Pachyrhinosaurini">Pachyrhinosaurini</a> (mostly had nasal bosses instead of horns)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Ornithopoda" title="Ornithopoda">Ornithopoda</a> (various sizes; bipeds and quadrupeds; evolved a method of chewing using skull flexibility and numerous teeth)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Jeholosauridae" title="Jeholosauridae">Jeholosauridae</a> (small Asian neornithischians)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Thescelosauridae" title="Thescelosauridae">Thescelosauridae</a> ("wondrous lizards")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Orodrominae" title="Orodrominae">Orodrominae</a> (burrowers)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Thescelosaurinae" title="Thescelosaurinae">Thescelosaurinae</a> (large thescelosaurids)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Iguanodontia" title="Iguanodontia">Iguanodontia</a> ("iguana teeth"; advanced ornithopods)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Elasmaria" title="Elasmaria">Elasmaria</a> (mostly southern ornithopods with mineralized plates along the ribs; may be thescelosaurids)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Rhabdodontomorpha" title="Rhabdodontomorpha">Rhabdodontomorpha</a> (with distinctive dentition)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Rhabdodontidae" title="Rhabdodontidae">Rhabdodontidae</a> (European rhabdodontomorphs)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Dryosauridae" title="Dryosauridae">Dryosauridae</a> (mid-sized, small headed)</li>
<li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camptosauridae" title="Camptosauridae">Camptosauridae</a> (mid-sized, stocky)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Styracosterna" title="Styracosterna">Styracosterna</a> ("spiked <a href="/wiki/Sternum" title="Sternum">sterna</a>")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadrosauriformes" title="Hadrosauriformes">Hadrosauriformes</a> (ancestrally had a thumb spike)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Hadrosauroidea" title="Hadrosauroidea">Hadrosauroidea</a> (large quadrupedal herbivores, with teeth merged into dental batteries)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Hadrosauromorpha" title="Hadrosauromorpha">Hadrosauromorpha</a> (hadrosaurids and their closest relatives)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Hadrosauridae" title="Hadrosauridae">Hadrosauridae</a> ("duck-billed dinosaurs"; often with crests)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Saurolophinae" title="Saurolophinae">Saurolophinae</a> (hadrosaurids with solid, small, no crests)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Brachylophosaurini" title="Brachylophosaurini">Brachylophosaurini</a> (short-crested)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Kritosaurini" title="Kritosaurini">Kritosaurini</a> (enlarged, solid nasal crests)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Saurolophini" title="Saurolophini">Saurolophini</a> (small, spike-like crests)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Edmontosaurini" title="Edmontosaurini">Edmontosaurini</a> (flat-headed saurolophines)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Lambeosaurinae" title="Lambeosaurinae">Lambeosaurinae</a> (hadrosaurids often with hollow crests)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Aralosaurini" title="Aralosaurini">Aralosaurini</a> (solid-crested)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Tsintaosaurini" title="Tsintaosaurini">Tsintaosaurini</a> (vertical, tube-like crests)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Parasaurolophini" title="Parasaurolophini">Parasaurolophini</a> (long, backwards-arcing crests)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Lambeosaurini" title="Lambeosaurini">Lambeosaurini</a> (usually rounded crests)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Saurischia" title="Saurischia">Saurischia</a></li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Herrerasauridae" title="Herrerasauridae">Herrerasauridae</a> (early bipedal carnivores)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="582" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg/220px-Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg/330px-Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg/440px-Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Restoration of four <a href="/wiki/Macronaria" title="Macronaria">macronarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">sauropods</a>: from left to right <i><a href="/wiki/Camarasaurus" title="Camarasaurus">Camarasaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Brachiosaurus" title="Brachiosaurus">Brachiosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Giraffatitan" title="Giraffatitan">Giraffatitan</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Euhelopus" title="Euhelopus">Euhelopus</a></i></div></div></div>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Sauropodomorpha" title="Sauropodomorpha">Sauropodomorpha</a> (herbivores with small heads, long necks, and long tails)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Unaysauridae" title="Unaysauridae">Unaysauridae</a> (primitive, strictly bipedal "prosauropods")</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Plateosauria" title="Plateosauria">Plateosauria</a> (diverse; bipeds and quadrupeds)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Massospondylidae" title="Massospondylidae">Massospondylidae</a> (long-necked, primitive sauropodomorphs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Riojasauridae" title="Riojasauridae">Riojasauridae</a> (large, primitive sauropodomorphs)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauropodiformes" title="Sauropodiformes">Sauropodiformes</a> (heavy, bipeds and quadrupeds)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">Sauropoda</a> (very large and heavy; quadrupedal)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Lessemsauridae" title="Lessemsauridae">Lessemsauridae</a> (gigantic yet lacking several weight-saving adaptations)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Gravisauria" title="Gravisauria">Gravisauria</a> ("heavy lizards")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Eusauropoda" title="Eusauropoda">Eusauropoda</a> ("true sauropods")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Turiasauria" title="Turiasauria">Turiasauria</a> (often large, widespread sauropods)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Neosauropoda" title="Neosauropoda">Neosauropoda</a> ("new sauropods"; columnar limbs)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Diplodocoidea" title="Diplodocoidea">Diplodocoidea</a> (skulls and tails elongated; teeth typically narrow and pencil-like)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Rebbachisauridae" title="Rebbachisauridae">Rebbachisauridae</a> (short-necked, low-browsing diplodocoids often with high backs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Flagellicaudata" title="Flagellicaudata">Flagellicaudata</a> (whip-tailed)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Dicraeosauridae" title="Dicraeosauridae">Dicraeosauridae</a> (small, short-necked diplodocoids with enlarged cervical and dorsal vertebrae)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Diplodocidae" title="Diplodocidae">Diplodocidae</a> (extremely long-necked)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Apatosaurinae" title="Apatosaurinae">Apatosaurinae</a> (robust cervical vertebrae)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Diplodocinae" title="Diplodocinae">Diplodocinae</a> (long, thin necks)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Macronaria" title="Macronaria">Macronaria</a> (boxy skulls; spoon- or pencil-shaped teeth)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanosauriformes" title="Titanosauriformes">Titanosauriformes</a> ("titan lizard forms")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Brachiosauridae" title="Brachiosauridae">Brachiosauridae</a> (long-necked, long-armed macronarians)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Somphospondyli" title="Somphospondyli">Somphospondyli</a> ("porous vertebrae")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Euhelopodidae" title="Euhelopodidae">Euhelopodidae</a> (stocky, mostly Asian)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Titanosauria" title="Titanosauria">Titanosauria</a> (diverse; stocky, with wide hips; most common in the Late Cretaceous of southern continents)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">Theropoda</a> (carnivorous)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Neotheropoda" title="Neotheropoda">Neotheropoda</a> ("new theropods")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Coelophysoidea" title="Coelophysoidea">Coelophysoidea</a> (early theropods; includes <i><a href="/wiki/Coelophysis" title="Coelophysis">Coelophysis</a></i> and close relatives)</li>
<li>†"Dilophosaur-grade neotheropods" (larger kink-snouted dinosaurs)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Averostra" title="Averostra">Averostra</a> ("bird snouts")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Ceratosauria" title="Ceratosauria">Ceratosauria</a> (generally elaborately horned carnivores that existed from the Jurassic to Cretaceous periods, originally included Coelophysoidea)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Ceratosauridae" title="Ceratosauridae">Ceratosauridae</a> (ceratosaurs with large teeth)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Abelisauroidea" title="Abelisauroidea">Abelisauroidea</a> (ceratosaurs exemplified by reduced arms and hands)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Abelisauridae" title="Abelisauridae">Abelisauridae</a> (large abelisauroids with short arms and oftentimes elaborate facial ornamentation)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Noasauridae" title="Noasauridae">Noasauridae</a> (diverse, generally light theropods; may include several obscure taxa)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elaphrosaurinae" title="Elaphrosaurinae">Elaphrosaurinae</a> (bird-like; omnivorous as juveniles but herbivorous as adults)</li>
<li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noasaurinae" title="Noasaurinae">Noasaurinae</a> (small carnivores)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Tetanurae" title="Tetanurae">Tetanurae</a> (stiff-tailed dinosaurs)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Megalosauroidea" title="Megalosauroidea">Megalosauroidea</a> (early group of large carnivores)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Piatnitzkysauridae" title="Piatnitzkysauridae">Piatnitzkysauridae</a> (small basal megalosauroids endemic to the Americas)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Megalosauridae" title="Megalosauridae">Megalosauridae</a> (large megalosauroids with powerful arms and hands)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Spinosauridae" title="Spinosauridae">Spinosauridae</a> (crocodile-like, semiaquatic carnivores)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Avetheropoda" title="Avetheropoda">Avetheropoda</a> ("bird theropods")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Megaraptora" title="Megaraptora">Megaraptora</a> (theropods with large hand claws; either carnosaurs or coelurosaurs, potentially tyrannosauroids)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Carnosauria" title="Carnosauria">Carnosauria</a> (large meat-eating dinosaurs; megalosauroids sometimes included)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Metriacanthosauridae" title="Metriacanthosauridae">Metriacanthosauridae</a> (primitive Asian allosauroids)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Allosauridae" title="Allosauridae">Allosauridae</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Allosaurus" title="Allosaurus">Allosaurus</a></i> and its very closest relatives)</li>
<li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carcharodontosauria" title="Carcharodontosauria">Carcharodontosauria</a> (robust allosauroids)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosauridae" title="Carcharodontosauridae">Carcharodontosauridae</a> (includes some of the largest purely terrestrial carnivores)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Neovenatoridae" title="Neovenatoridae">Neovenatoridae</a> ("new hunters"; may include megaraptorans)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Coelurosauria" title="Coelurosauria">Coelurosauria</a> (feathered theropods, with a range of body sizes and niches)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†"Nexus of basal coelurosaurs" (used by Tweet to denote well-known taxa with unstable positions at the base of Coelurosauria)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Tyrannoraptora" title="Tyrannoraptora">Tyrannoraptora</a> ("tyrant thieves")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Compsognathidae" title="Compsognathidae">Compsognathidae</a> (small early coelurosaurs with short forelimbs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauroidea" title="Tyrannosauroidea">Tyrannosauroidea</a> (mostly large, primitive coelurosaurs)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Proceratosauridae" title="Proceratosauridae">Proceratosauridae</a> (tyrannosauroids with head crests)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">Tyrannosauridae</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i> and close relatives)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Maniraptoriformes" title="Maniraptoriformes">Maniraptoriformes</a> (bird-like dinosaurs)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Ornithomimosauria" title="Ornithomimosauria">Ornithomimosauria</a> (small-headed, mostly toothless, omnivorous or possible herbivores)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Ornithomimidae" title="Ornithomimidae">Ornithomimidae</a> (very ostrich-like dinosaurs)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">Maniraptora</a> (dinosaurs with pennaceous feathers)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dromaeosaurs.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="6369" decoding="async" height="81" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dromaeosaurs.png/220px-Dromaeosaurs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dromaeosaurs.png/330px-Dromaeosaurs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dromaeosaurs.png/440px-Dromaeosaurs.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dromaeosaurs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Paleoart" title="Paleoart">Restoration</a> of six <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">dromaeosaurid</a> <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a>: from left to right <i><a href="/wiki/Microraptor" title="Microraptor">Microraptor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Velociraptor" title="Velociraptor">Velociraptor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Austroraptor" title="Austroraptor">Austroraptor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dromaeosaurus" title="Dromaeosaurus">Dromaeosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Utahraptor" title="Utahraptor">Utahraptor</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Deinonychus" title="Deinonychus">Deinonychus</a></i></div></div></div>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Alvarezsauroidea" title="Alvarezsauroidea">Alvarezsauroidea</a> (small hunters with reduced forelimbs)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Alvarezsauridae" title="Alvarezsauridae">Alvarezsauridae</a> (insectivores with only one enlarged digit)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Therizinosauria" title="Therizinosauria">Therizinosauria</a> (tall, long-necked theropods; omnivores and herbivores)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therizinosauroidea" title="Therizinosauroidea">Therizinosauroidea</a> (larger therizinosaurs)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Therizinosauridae" title="Therizinosauridae">Therizinosauridae</a> (sloth-like herbivores, often with enlarged claws)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Oviraptorosauria" title="Oviraptorosauria">Oviraptorosauria</a> (omnivorous, beaked dinosaurs)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Caudipteridae" title="Caudipteridae">Caudipteridae</a> (bird-like, basal oviraptorosaurs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Caenagnathoidea" title="Caenagnathoidea">Caenagnathoidea</a> (cassowary-like oviraptorosaurs)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Caenagnathidae" title="Caenagnathidae">Caenagnathidae</a> (toothless oviraptorosaurs known from North America and Asia)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Oviraptoridae" title="Oviraptoridae">Oviraptoridae</a> (characterized by two bony projections at the back of the mouth; exclusive to Asia)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Paraves" title="Paraves">Paraves</a> (avialans and their closest relatives)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Scansoriopterygidae" title="Scansoriopterygidae">Scansoriopterygidae</a> (small tree-climbing theropods with membranous wings)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Deinonychosauria" title="Deinonychosauria">Deinonychosauria</a> (toe-clawed dinosaurs; may not form a natural group)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Archaeopterygidae" title="Archaeopterygidae">Archaeopterygidae</a> (small, winged theropods or primitive birds)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Troodontidae" title="Troodontidae">Troodontidae</a> (omnivores; enlarged brain cavities)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">Dromaeosauridae</a> ("raptors")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Microraptoria" title="Microraptoria">Microraptoria</a> (characterized by large wings on both the arms and legs; may have been capable of powered flight)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Eudromaeosauria" title="Eudromaeosauria">Eudromaeosauria</a> (hunters with greatly enlarged sickle claws)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Unenlagiidae" title="Unenlagiidae">Unenlagiidae</a> (piscivores; may be dromaeosaurids)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Halszkaraptorinae" title="Halszkaraptorinae">Halszkaraptorinae</a> (duck-like; potentially semiaquatic)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Unenlagiinae" title="Unenlagiinae">Unenlagiinae</a> (long-snouted)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Avialae" title="Avialae">Avialae</a> (modern birds and extinct relatives)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>

alt="Diplodocoidea" coords="616,182,741,206" href="/wiki/Diplodocoidea" shape="rect" title="Diplodocoidea"/><area alt="Cetiosauridae" coords="394,168,519,192" href="/wiki/Cetiosauridae" shape="rect" title="Cetiosauridae"/><area alt="Turiasauria" coords="491,154,603,178" href="/wiki/Turiasauria" shape="rect" title="Turiasauria"/><area alt="Vulcanodontidae" coords="296,139,435,163" href="/wiki/Vulcanodontidae" shape="rect" title="Vulcanodontidae"/><area alt="Massospondyildae" coords="292,125,437,149" href="/wiki/Massospondyildae" shape="rect" title="Massospondyildae"/><area alt="Riojasauridae" coords="148,111,273,135" href="/wiki/Riojasauridae" shape="rect" title="Riojasauridae"/><area alt="Plateosauridae" coords="238,96,369,120" href="/wiki/Plateosauridae" shape="rect" title="Plateosauridae"/><area alt="Guaibasauridae" coords="167,82,299,106" href="/wiki/Guaibasauridae" shape="rect" title="Guaibasauridae"/><area alt="Herrerasauridae" coords="160,68,298,92" href="/wiki/Herrerasauridae" shape="rect" title="Herrerasauridae"/><area alt="Quaternary" coords="958,11,1010,35" href="/wiki/Quaternary" shape="rect" title="Quaternary"/><area alt="Neogene" coords="896,11,981,35" href="/wiki/Neogene" shape="rect" title="Neogene"/><area alt="Paleogene" coords="767,11,865,35" href="/wiki/Paleogene" shape="rect" title="Paleogene"/><area alt="Cretaceous" coords="64,11,155,35" href="/wiki/Triassic" shape="rect" title="Triassic"/><area alt="Holocene" coords="963,40,1015,64" href="/wiki/Holocene" shape="rect" title="Holocene"/><area alt="Pleistocene" coords="901,40,966,64" href="/wiki/Miocene" shape="rect" title="Miocene"/><area alt="Oligocene" coords="839,40,917,64" href="/wiki/Oligocene" shape="rect" title="Oligocene"/><area alt="Eocene" Cretaceous"/><area alt="Late Jurassic" coords="358,40,423,64" href="/wiki/Late_Jurassic" shape="rect" title="Late Jurassic"/><area alt="Middle Jurassic" coords="292,40,371,64" Triassic" coords="122,40,187,64" href="/wiki/Late_Triassic" shape="rect" title="Late Triassic"/><area alt="Middle Triassic" coords="26,40,105,64" href="/wiki/Middle_Triassic" shape="rect" title="Middle Triassic"/><area alt="Early Triassic" coords="-11,40,53,64" href="/wiki/Early_Triassic" shape="rect" title="Early Triassic"/></map><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/hy7o3va3f3rottsqki0x14txja1gy07.png" usemap="#timeline_hy7o3va3f3rottsqki0x14txja1gy07"/></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleobiology">Paleobiology</span></h2>
<p>Knowledge about dinosaurs is derived from a variety of fossil and non-fossil records, including fossilized bones, <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_trackway" title="Fossil trackway">trackways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gastrolith" title="Gastrolith">gastroliths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feathers</a>, impressions of skin, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">internal organs</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Soft_tissue" title="Soft tissue">soft tissues</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-softtissue_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-softtissue-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzer2005_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer2005-66">[65]</a></sup> Many fields of study contribute to our understanding of dinosaurs, including <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">biomechanics</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth sciences</a> (of which <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a> is a sub-discipline).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alexander2006_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-alexander2006-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinobiology_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-dinobiology-104">[103]</a></sup> Two topics of particular interest and study have been dinosaur size and behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_size" title="Dinosaur size">Dinosaur size</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Longest_dinosaur_by_clade.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1150" data-file-width="3360" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Longest_dinosaur_by_clade.svg/320px-Longest_dinosaur_by_clade.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Longest_dinosaur_by_clade.svg/480px-Longest_dinosaur_by_clade.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Longest_dinosaur_by_clade.svg/640px-Longest_dinosaur_by_clade.svg.png 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Longest_dinosaur_by_clade.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scale diagram comparing the average human to the longest known dinosaurs in five major <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a>:<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ae2032; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">Sauropoda</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Supersaurus" title="Supersaurus">Supersaurus vivianae</a></i>)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#cc48cc; color:black;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod">Ornithopoda</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Shantungosaurus" title="Shantungosaurus">Shantungosaurus giganteus</a></i>)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#50da50; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">Theropoda</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Spinosaurus" title="Spinosaurus">Spinosaurus aegyptiacus</a></i>)</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f8863b; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Thyreophora" title="Thyreophora">Thyreophora</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Stegosaurus" title="Stegosaurus">Stegosaurus ungulatus</a></i>)</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#254c87; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Marginocephalia" title="Marginocephalia">Marginocephalia</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops prorsus</a></i>)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Current evidence suggests that dinosaur average size varied through the Triassic, Early Jurassic, Late Jurassic and Cretaceous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sereno1999_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sereno1999-80">[79]</a></sup> Predatory theropod dinosaurs, which occupied most terrestrial carnivore niches during the Mesozoic, most often fall into the 100 to <span style="white-space: nowrap">1<span style="margin-left: 0.25em">000</span></span> kg (220 to <span style="white-space: nowrap">2<span style="margin-left: 0.25em">200</span></span> lb) category when sorted by estimated weight into categories based on <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">order of magnitude</a>, whereas <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">recent</a> predatory carnivoran mammals peak in the 10 to 100 kg (22 to 220 lb) category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JF93_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-JF93-106">[105]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mode_(statistics)" title="Mode (statistics)">mode</a> of Mesozoic dinosaur body masses is between 1 to 10 metric tons (1.1 to 11.0 short tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peczkis1994_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peczkis1994-107">[106]</a></sup> This contrasts sharply with the average size of Cenozoic mammals, estimated by the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="National Museum of Natural History">National Museum of Natural History</a> as about 2 to 5 kg (4.4 to 11.0 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMNH_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMNH-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sauropods were the largest and heaviest dinosaurs. For much of the dinosaur era, the smallest sauropods were larger than anything else in their habitat, and the largest was an order of magnitude more massive than anything else that has since walked the Earth. Giant prehistoric mammals such as <i><a href="/wiki/Paraceratherium" title="Paraceratherium">Paraceratherium</a></i> (the largest land mammal ever) were dwarfed by the giant sauropods, and only modern whales approach or surpass them in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sander2011_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-sander2011-109">[108]</a></sup> There are several proposed advantages for the large size of sauropods, including protection from predation, reduction of energy use, and longevity, but it may be that the most important advantage was dietary. Large animals are more efficient at digestion than small animals, because food spends more time in their digestive systems. This also permits them to subsist on food with lower nutritive value than smaller animals. Sauropod remains are mostly found in rock formations interpreted as dry or seasonally dry, and the ability to eat large quantities of low-nutrient browse would have been advantageous in such environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC06_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-KC06-110">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Largest_and_smallest">Largest and smallest</span></h4>
<p>Scientists will probably never be certain of the <a href="/wiki/Largest_organisms" title="Largest organisms">largest and smallest dinosaurs</a> to have ever existed. This is because only a tiny percentage of animals were ever fossilized and most of these remain buried in the earth. Few of the specimens that are recovered are complete skeletons, and impressions of skin and other soft tissues are rare. Rebuilding a complete skeleton by comparing the size and morphology of bones to those of similar, better-known species is an inexact art, and reconstructing the muscles and other organs of the living animal is, at best, a process of educated guesswork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Argentinosaurus_9.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="13650" data-file-width="33384" decoding="async" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Argentinosaurus_9.svg/220px-Argentinosaurus_9.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Argentinosaurus_9.svg/330px-Argentinosaurus_9.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Argentinosaurus_9.svg/440px-Argentinosaurus_9.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Argentinosaurus_9.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparative size of <i><a href="/wiki/Argentinosaurus" title="Argentinosaurus">Argentinosaurus</a></i> to the average human</div></div></div>
<p>The tallest and heaviest dinosaur known from good skeletons is <i><a href="/wiki/Giraffatitan" title="Giraffatitan">Giraffatitan brancai</a></i> (previously classified as a species of <i><a href="/wiki/Brachiosaurus" title="Brachiosaurus">Brachiosaurus</a></i>). Its remains were discovered in Tanzania between 1907 and 1912. Bones from several similar-sized individuals were incorporated into the skeleton now mounted and on display at the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_Berlin" title="Natural History Museum, Berlin">Museum für Naturkunde</a> in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC68_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-EC68-112">[111]</a></sup> this mount is 12 meters (39 ft) tall and 21.8 to 22.5 meters (72 to 74 ft) long,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> and would have belonged to an animal that weighed between <span style="white-space:nowrap">30<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> and <span style="white-space:nowrap">60<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> kilograms (<span style="white-space:nowrap">70<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> and <span style="white-space:nowrap">130<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> lb). The longest complete dinosaur is the 27 meters (89 ft) long <i>Diplodocus</i>, which was discovered in <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and displayed in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Carnegie Museum of Natural History">Carnegie Museum of Natural History</a> in 1907.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lucas04_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-lucas04-115">[114]</a></sup> The longest dinosaur known from good fossil material is the <i><a href="/wiki/Patagotitan" title="Patagotitan">Patagotitan</a></i>: the skeleton mount in the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> is 37 meters (121 ft) long. The <a href="/wiki/Museo_Carmen_Funes" title="Museo Carmen Funes">Museo Municipal Carmen Funes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Plaza_Huincul" title="Plaza Huincul">Plaza Huincul</a>, Argentina, has an <i><a href="/wiki/Argentinosaurus" title="Argentinosaurus">Argentinosaurus</a></i> reconstructed skeleton mount that is 39.7 meters (130 ft) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plosone_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-plosone-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bee_hummingbird_(Mellisuga_helenae)_adult_male_in_flight-cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2301" data-file-width="2334" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Bee_hummingbird_%28Mellisuga_helenae%29_adult_male_in_flight-cropped.jpg/220px-Bee_hummingbird_%28Mellisuga_helenae%29_adult_male_in_flight-cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Bee_hummingbird_%28Mellisuga_helenae%29_adult_male_in_flight-cropped.jpg/330px-Bee_hummingbird_%28Mellisuga_helenae%29_adult_male_in_flight-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Bee_hummingbird_%28Mellisuga_helenae%29_adult_male_in_flight-cropped.jpg/440px-Bee_hummingbird_%28Mellisuga_helenae%29_adult_male_in_flight-cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bee_hummingbird_(Mellisuga_helenae)_adult_male_in_flight-cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult <a href="/wiki/Bee_hummingbird" title="Bee hummingbird">bee hummingbird</a>, the smallest known dinosaur</div></div></div>
<p>There were larger dinosaurs, but knowledge of them is based entirely on a small number of fragmentary fossils. Most of the largest herbivorous specimens on record were discovered in the 1970s or later, and include the massive <i>Argentinosaurus</i>, which may have weighed <span style="white-space:nowrap">80<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> to <span style="white-space:nowrap">100<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> kilograms (90 to 110 short tons) and reached lengths of 30 to 40 meters (98 to 131 ft); some of the longest were the 33.5-meter (110 ft) long <i>Diplodocus hallorum</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC06_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-KC06-110">[109]</a></sup> (formerly <i>Seismosaurus</i>), the 33-to-34-meter (108 to 112 ft) long <i><a href="/wiki/Supersaurus" title="Supersaurus">Supersaurus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LHW07_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-LHW07-117">[116]</a></sup> and 37-meter (121 ft) long <i>Patagotitan</i>; and the tallest, the 18-meter (59 ft) tall <i><a href="/wiki/Sauroposeidon" title="Sauroposeidon">Sauroposeidon</a></i>, which could have reached a sixth-floor window. The heaviest and longest dinosaur may have been <i><a href="/wiki/Maraapunisaurus" title="Maraapunisaurus">Maraapunisaurus</a></i>, known only from a now lost partial vertebral <a href="/wiki/Vertebra#Structure" title="Vertebra">neural arch</a> described in 1878. Extrapolating from the illustration of this bone, the animal may have been 58 meters (190 ft) long and weighed <span style="white-space:nowrap">122<span style="margin-left:0.25em">400</span></span> kg (<span style="white-space:nowrap">270<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC06_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-KC06-110">[109]</a></sup> However, as no further evidence of sauropods of this size has been found, and the discoverer, Cope, had made typographic errors before, it is likely to have been an extreme overestimation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest carnivorous dinosaur was <i><a href="/wiki/Spinosaurus" title="Spinosaurus">Spinosaurus</a></i>, reaching a length of 12.6 to 18 meters (41 to 59 ft), and weighing 7 to 20.9 metric tons (7.7 to 23.0 short tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMBM06_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMBM06-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-TH07-120">[119]</a></sup> Other large carnivorous theropods included <i><a href="/wiki/Giganotosaurus" title="Giganotosaurus">Giganotosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosaurus" title="Carcharodontosaurus">Carcharodontosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-TH07-120">[119]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Therizinosaurus" title="Therizinosaurus">Therizinosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Deinocheirus" title="Deinocheirus">Deinocheirus</a></i> were among the tallest of the theropods. The largest ornithischian dinosaur was probably the hadrosaurid <i><a href="/wiki/Shantungosaurus" title="Shantungosaurus">Shantungosaurus giganteus</a></i> which measured 16.6 meters (54 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> The largest individuals may have weighed as much as 16 metric tons (18 short tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The smallest dinosaur known is the <a href="/wiki/Bee_hummingbird" title="Bee hummingbird">bee hummingbird</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> with a length of only 5 centimeters (2.0 in) and mass of around 1.8 g (0.063 oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> The smallest known non-<a href="/wiki/Avialae" title="Avialae">avialan</a> dinosaurs were about the size of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigeon" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a> and were those theropods most closely related to birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhang2008_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhang2008-125">[124]</a></sup> For example, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchiornis_huxleyi" title="Anchiornis huxleyi">Anchiornis huxleyi</a></i> is currently the smallest non-avialan dinosaur described from an adult specimen, with an estimated weight of 110 g (3.9 oz)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anchiadvance_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-anchiadvance-126">[125]</a></sup> and a total skeletal length of 34 centimeters (1.12 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhang2008_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-zhang2008-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anchiadvance_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-anchiadvance-126">[125]</a></sup> The smallest herbivorous non-avialan dinosaurs included <i><a href="/wiki/Microceratus" title="Microceratus">Microceratus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Wannanosaurus" title="Wannanosaurus">Wannanosaurus</a></i>, at about 60 centimeters (2.0 ft) long each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holtz2007_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holtz2007-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-butler&amp;zhao2009_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-butler&amp;zhao2009-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maiasaurusnest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Maiasaurusnest.jpg/220px-Maiasaurusnest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Maiasaurusnest.jpg/330px-Maiasaurusnest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Maiasaurusnest.jpg/440px-Maiasaurusnest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maiasaurusnest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A nesting ground of the hadrosaur <i><a href="/wiki/Maiasaura" title="Maiasaura">Maiasaura peeblesorum</a></i> was discovered in 1978</div></div></div>
<p>Many modern birds are highly social, often found living in flocks. There is general agreement that some behaviors that are common in birds, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a> (closest living relatives of birds), were also common among extinct dinosaur groups. Interpretations of behavior in fossil species are generally based on the pose of skeletons and their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" title="Habitat (ecology)">habitat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">computer simulations</a> of their biomechanics, and comparisons with modern animals in similar ecological niches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alexander2006_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-alexander2006-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first potential evidence for <a href="/wiki/Herd" title="Herd">herding</a> or <a href="/wiki/Flocking_(behavior)" title="Flocking (behavior)">flocking</a> as a widespread behavior common to many dinosaur groups in addition to birds was the 1878 discovery of 31 <i>Iguanodon</i>, ornithischians that were then thought to have perished together in <a href="/wiki/Bernissart" title="Bernissart">Bernissart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, after they fell into a deep, flooded <a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">sinkhole</a> and drowned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yans_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yans-129">[128]</a></sup> Other mass-death sites have been discovered subsequently. Those, along with multiple trackways, suggest that gregarious behavior was common in many early dinosaur species. Trackways of hundreds or even thousands of herbivores indicate that duck-billed (hadrosaurids) may have moved in great herds, like the <a href="/wiki/American_bison" title="American bison">American bison</a> or the African <a href="/wiki/Springbok" title="Springbok">Springbok</a>. Sauropod tracks document that these animals traveled in groups composed of several different species, at least in <a href="/wiki/Oxfordshire" title="Oxfordshire">Oxfordshire</a>, England,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day2002_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day2002-130">[129]</a></sup> although there is no evidence for specific herd structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JLW05_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-JLW05-131">[130]</a></sup> Congregating into herds may have evolved for defense, for <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a> purposes, or to provide protection for young. There is evidence that many types of slow-growing dinosaurs, including various theropods, sauropods, ankylosaurians, ornithopods, and ceratopsians, formed aggregations of immature individuals. One example is a site in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> that has yielded remains of over 20 <i><a href="/wiki/Sinornithomimus" title="Sinornithomimus">Sinornithomimus</a></i>, from one to seven years old. This assemblage is interpreted as a social group that was trapped in mud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DVetal08sino_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-DVetal08sino-132">[131]</a></sup> The interpretation of dinosaurs as gregarious has also extended to depicting carnivorous theropods as <a href="/wiki/Pack_hunter" title="Pack hunter">pack hunters</a> working together to bring down large prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LG93_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-LG93-133">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxwell&amp;ostrom1995_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-maxwell&amp;ostrom1995-134">[133]</a></sup> However, this lifestyle is uncommon among modern birds, crocodiles, and other reptiles, and the <a href="/wiki/Taphonomy" title="Taphonomy">taphonomic</a> evidence suggesting mammal-like pack hunting in such theropods as <i>Deinonychus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Allosaurus" title="Allosaurus">Allosaurus</a></i> can also be interpreted as the results of fatal disputes between feeding animals, as is seen in many modern diapsid predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RB07_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-RB07-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="808" data-file-width="1700" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png/220px-Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png/330px-Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png/440px-Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Restoration of two <i><a href="/wiki/Centrosaurus" title="Centrosaurus">Centrosaurus apertus</a></i> engaged in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intra-specific_combat" title="Intra-specific combat">intra-specific combat</a></div></div></div>
<p>The crests and frills of some dinosaurs, like the <a href="/wiki/Marginocephalia" title="Marginocephalia">marginocephalians</a>, theropods and <a href="/wiki/Lambeosaurinae" title="Lambeosaurinae">lambeosaurines</a>, may have been too fragile to be used for active defense, and so they were likely used for sexual or aggressive displays, though little is known about dinosaur mating and <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territorialism</a>. Head wounds from bites suggest that theropods, at least, engaged in active aggressive confrontations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PC98_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-PC98-136">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>From a behavioral standpoint, one of the most valuable dinosaur fossils was discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Desert" title="Gobi Desert">Gobi Desert</a> in 1971. It included a <i><a href="/wiki/Velociraptor" title="Velociraptor">Velociraptor</a></i> attacking a <i><a href="/wiki/Protoceratops" title="Protoceratops">Protoceratops</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMNH_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMNH-137">[136]</a></sup> providing evidence that dinosaurs did indeed attack each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpenter1998_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-carpenter1998-138">[137]</a></sup> Additional evidence for attacking live prey is the partially healed tail of an <i><a href="/wiki/Edmontosaurus" title="Edmontosaurus">Edmontosaurus</a></i>, a hadrosaurid dinosaur; the tail is damaged in such a way that shows the animal was bitten by a tyrannosaur but survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpenter1998_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-carpenter1998-138">[137]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cannibalism_(zoology)" title="Cannibalism (zoology)">Cannibalism</a> amongst some species of dinosaurs was confirmed by tooth marks found in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> in 2003, involving the theropod <i><a href="/wiki/Majungasaurus" title="Majungasaurus">Majungasaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogersetal2003_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-rogersetal2003-139">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Comparisons between the <a href="/wiki/Sclerotic_ring" title="Sclerotic ring">scleral rings</a> of dinosaurs and modern birds and reptiles have been used to infer daily activity patterns of dinosaurs. Although it has been suggested that most dinosaurs were active during the day, these comparisons have shown that small predatory dinosaurs such as dromaeosaurids, <i><a href="/wiki/Juravenator" title="Juravenator">Juravenator</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Megapnosaurus" title="Megapnosaurus">Megapnosaurus</a></i> were likely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nocturnal" title="Nocturnal">nocturnal</a>. Large and medium-sized herbivorous and omnivorous dinosaurs such as ceratopsians, sauropodomorphs, hadrosaurids, ornithomimosaurs may have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathemeral" title="Cathemeral">cathemeral</a>, active during short intervals throughout the day, although the small ornithischian <i><a href="/wiki/Agilisaurus" title="Agilisaurus">Agilisaurus</a></i> was inferred to be <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchmitzMotani2011_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchmitzMotani2011-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on fossil evidence from dinosaurs such as <i><a href="/wiki/Oryctodromeus" title="Oryctodromeus">Oryctodromeus</a></i>, some ornithischian species seem to have led a partially <a href="/wiki/Fossorial" title="Fossorial">fossorial</a> (burrowing) lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VMK07_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-VMK07-141">[140]</a></sup> Many modern birds are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arboreal" title="Arboreal">arboreal</a> (tree climbing), and this was also true of many Mesozoic birds, especially the enantiornithines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> While some early bird-like species may have already been arboreal as well (including dromaeosaurids) such as <i>Microraptor</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chatterjee2007_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-chatterjee2007-143">[142]</a></sup>) most non-avialan dinosaurs seem to have relied on land-based locomotion. A good understanding of how dinosaurs moved on the ground is key to models of dinosaur behavior; the science of biomechanics, pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Robert_McNeill_Alexander" title="Robert McNeill Alexander">Robert McNeill Alexander</a>, has provided significant insight in this area. For example, studies of the forces exerted by muscles and gravity on dinosaurs' skeletal structure have investigated how fast dinosaurs could run,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alexander2006_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-alexander2006-103">[102]</a></sup> whether <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diplodocid" title="Diplodocid">diplodocids</a> could create <a href="/wiki/Sonic_boom" title="Sonic boom">sonic booms</a> via <a href="/wiki/Whip" title="Whip">whip</a>-like tail snapping,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goriely_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-goriely-144">[143]</a></sup> and whether sauropods could float.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson2006_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson2006-145">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg/220px-Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg/330px-Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg/440px-Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Restoration of a striking and unusual visual display in a <i><a href="/wiki/Lambeosaurus" title="Lambeosaurus">Lambeosaurus magnicristatus</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Modern birds are known to <a href="/wiki/Animal_communication" title="Animal communication">communicate</a> using visual and auditory signals, and the wide diversity of visual display structures among fossil dinosaur groups, such as horns, frills, crests, sails, and feathers, suggests that visual communication has always been important in dinosaur biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senter2008_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-senter2008-146">[145]</a></sup> Reconstruction of the plumage color of <i>Anchiornis</i>, suggest the importance of color in visual communication in non-avian dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2010_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li2010-147">[146]</a></sup> The evolution of dinosaur vocalization is less certain. Paleontologist Phil Senter has suggested that non-avian dinosaurs relied mostly on visual displays and possibly non-vocal acoustic sounds like hissing, jaw grinding or clapping, splashing and wing beating (possible in winged maniraptoran dinosaurs). He states they were unlikely to have been capable of vocalizing since their closest relatives, crocodilians and birds, use different means to vocalize, the former via the <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a> and the latter through the unique <a href="/wiki/Syrinx_(bird_anatomy)" title="Syrinx (bird anatomy)">syrinx</a>, suggesting they evolved independently and their common ancestor was mute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senter2008_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-senter2008-146">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest remains of a syrinx, which has enough mineral content for fossilization, was found in a specimen of the duck-like <i><a href="/wiki/Vegavis" title="Vegavis">Vegavis iaai</a></i> dated 69 –66 million years ago, and this organ is unlikely to have existed in non-avian dinosaurs. However, in contrast to Senter, other researchers have suggested that dinosaurs could vocalize and that the syrinx-based vocal system of birds evolved from a larynx-based one, rather than the two systems evolving independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke2016_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke2016-148">[147]</a></sup> A 2016 study suggests that some dinosaurs produced closed mouth vocalizations like cooing, hooting and booming. These occur in both reptiles and birds and involve inflating the esophagus or tracheal pouches. Such vocalizations evolved independently in extant archosaurs numerous times, following increases in body size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobias2016_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tobias2016-149">[148]</a></sup> The crests of the <a href="/wiki/Lambeosaurini" title="Lambeosaurini">Lambeosaurini</a> and nasal chambers of ankylosaurids have been suggested to have functioned in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vocal_resonance" title="Vocal resonance">vocal resonance</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weishampel981_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weishampel981-150">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witmer_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witmer-151">[150]</a></sup> though Senter stated that the presence of resonance chambers in some dinosaurs is not necessarily evidence of vocalization as modern snakes have such chambers which intensify their hisses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senter2008_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-senter2008-146">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_biology">Reproductive biology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_egg" title="Dinosaur egg">Dinosaur egg</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG"><img alt="Three bluish eggs with black speckling sit atop a layer of white mollusk shell pieces, surrounded by sandy ground and small bits of bluish stone" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG/220px-Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG/330px-Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG/440px-Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nest of a <a href="/wiki/Plover" title="Plover">plover</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Charadrius" title="Charadrius">Charadrius</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>All dinosaurs laid <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotic eggs</a>. Dinosaur eggs were usually laid in a nest. Most species create somewhat elaborate nests which can be cups, domes, plates, beds scrapes, mounds, or burrows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansell_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansell-152">[151]</a></sup> Some species of modern bird have no nests; the cliff-nesting <a href="/wiki/Common_murre" title="Common murre">common guillemot</a> lays its eggs on bare rock, and male <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguins</a> keep eggs between their body and feet. Primitive birds and many non-avialan dinosaurs often lay eggs in communal nests, with males primarily incubating the eggs. While modern birds have only one functional <a href="/wiki/Oviduct" title="Oviduct">oviduct</a> and lay one egg at a time, more primitive birds and dinosaurs had two oviducts, like crocodiles. Some non-avialan dinosaurs, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Troodon" title="Troodon">Troodon</a></i>, exhibited iterative laying, where the adult might lay a pair of eggs every one or two days, and then ensured simultaneous hatching by delaying <a href="/wiki/Broodiness#Broodiness_in_non-avian_animals" title="Broodiness">brooding</a> until all eggs were laid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varrichioetal.02_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Varrichioetal.02-153">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>When laying eggs, females grow a special type of bone between the hard outer bone and the <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">marrow</a> of their limbs. This medullary bone, which is rich in <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, is used to make eggshells. A discovery of features in a <i>Tyrannosaurus</i> skeleton provided evidence of medullary bone in extinct dinosaurs and, for the first time, allowed paleontologists to establish the sex of a fossil dinosaur specimen. Further research has found medullary bone in the carnosaur <i>Allosaurus</i> and the ornithopod <i><a href="/wiki/Tenontosaurus" title="Tenontosaurus">Tenontosaurus</a></i>. Because the line of dinosaurs that includes <i>Allosaurus</i> and <i>Tyrannosaurus</i> diverged from the line that led to <i>Tenontosaurus</i> very early in the evolution of dinosaurs, this suggests that the production of medullary tissue is a general characteristic of all dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LW08_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-LW08-154">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg/220px-Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg/330px-Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg/440px-Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fossil interpreted as a nesting <a href="/wiki/Oviraptoridae" title="Oviraptoridae">oviraptorid</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Citipati" title="Citipati">Citipati</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a>. Smaller fossil far right showing inside one of the eggs.</div></div></div>
<p>Another widespread trait among modern birds (but see below in regards to fossil groups and extant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megapodes" title="Megapodes">megapodes</a>) is parental care for young after hatching. <a href="/wiki/Jack_Horner_(paleontologist)" title="Jack Horner (paleontologist)">Jack Horner's</a> 1978 discovery of a <i><a href="/wiki/Maiasaura" title="Maiasaura">Maiasaura</a></i> ("good mother lizard") nesting ground in Montana demonstrated that parental care continued long after birth among ornithopods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HM79_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-HM79-155">[154]</a></sup> A specimen of the <a href="/wiki/Oviraptoridae" title="Oviraptoridae">oviraptorid</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Citipati" title="Citipati">Citipati osmolskae</a></i> was discovered in a <a href="/wiki/Chicken#Broodiness" title="Chicken">chicken-like brooding position</a> in 1993,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-search.eb_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-search.eb-156">[155]</a></sup> which may indicate that they had begun using an insulating layer of feathers to keep the eggs warm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> An embryo of the basal sauropodomorph <i><a href="/wiki/Massospondylus" title="Massospondylus">Massospondylus</a></i> was found without teeth, indicating that some parental care was required to feed the young dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reiszetal05_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reiszetal05-158">[157]</a></sup> Trackways have also confirmed parental behavior among ornithopods from the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" title="Isle of Skye">Isle of Skye</a> in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCtracks_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCtracks-159">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, there is ample evidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precociality" title="Precociality">precociality</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precociality#Superprecociality" title="Precociality">superprecociality</a> among many dinosaur species, particularly theropods. For instance, non-<a href="/wiki/Euornithes" title="Euornithes">ornithuromorph</a> birds have been abundantly demonstrated to have had slow growth rates, <a href="/wiki/Megapode" title="Megapode">megapode</a>-like egg burying behavior and the ability to fly soon after birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> Both <i>Tyrannosaurus</i> and <i>Troodon</i> had juveniles with clear superprecociality and likely occupying different ecological niches than the adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varrichioetal.02_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Varrichioetal.02-153">[152]</a></sup> Superprecociality has been inferred for sauropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Genital structures are unlikely to fossilize as they lack scales that may allow preservation via pigmentation or residual calcium phosphate salts. In 2021, the best preserved specimen of a dinosaur's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloacal_vent" title="Cloacal vent">cloacal vent</a> exterior was described for <i><a href="/wiki/Psittacosaurus" title="Psittacosaurus">Psittacosaurus</a></i>, demonstrating lateral swellings similar to crocodylian musk glands used in social displays by both sexes and pigmented regions which could also reflect a signalling function. However, this specimen on its own does not offer enough information to determine whether this dinosaur had sexual signalling functions; it only supports the possibility. Cloacal visual signalling can occur in either males or females in living birds, making it unlikely to be useful to determine sex for extinct dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloacal-vent_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-cloacal-vent-165">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Physiology_of_dinosaurs" title="Physiology of dinosaurs">Physiology of dinosaurs</a></div>
<p>Because both modern crocodilians and birds have four-chambered hearts (albeit modified in crocodilians), it is likely that this is a trait shared by all archosaurs, including all dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CH04_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-CH04-166">[165]</a></sup> While all modern birds have high metabolisms and are <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">endothermic</a> ("warm-blooded"), a vigorous debate has been ongoing since the 1960s regarding how far back in the dinosaur lineage this trait extended. Various researchers have supported dinosaurs as being endothermic, <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">ectothermic</a> ("cold-blooded"), or somewhere in between.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pontzer2009_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-pontzer2009-167">[166]</a></sup> An emerging consensus among researchers is that, while different lineages of dinosaurs would have had different metabolisms, most of them had higher metabolic rates than other reptiles but lower than living birds and mammals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson2018_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-benson2018-168">[167]</a></sup> which is termed <a href="/wiki/Mesotherm" title="Mesotherm">mesothermy</a> by some.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grady2014_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-grady2014-169">[168]</a></sup> Evidence from crocodiles and their extinct relatives suggests that such elevated metabolisms could have developed in the earliest archosaurs, which were the common ancestors of dinosaurs and crocodiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-legendre2016_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-legendre2016-170">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seymour2004_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-seymour2004-171">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pasta-Brontosaurus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3056" data-file-width="4655" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Pasta-Brontosaurus.jpg/220px-Pasta-Brontosaurus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Pasta-Brontosaurus.jpg/330px-Pasta-Brontosaurus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Pasta-Brontosaurus.jpg/440px-Pasta-Brontosaurus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pasta-Brontosaurus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This 1897 restoration of <i><a href="/wiki/Brontosaurus" title="Brontosaurus">Brontosaurus</a></i> as an aquatic, tail-dragging animal, by <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Knight" title="Charles R. Knight">Charles R. Knight</a>, typified early views on dinosaur lifestyles.</div></div></div>
<p>After non-avian dinosaurs were discovered, paleontologists first posited that they were ectothermic. This was used to imply that the ancient dinosaurs were relatively slow, sluggish organisms, even though many modern reptiles are fast and light-footed despite relying on external sources of heat to regulate their body temperature. The idea of dinosaurs as ectothermic remained a prevalent view until <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._Bakker" title="Robert T. Bakker">Robert T. Bakker</a>, an early proponent of dinosaur endothermy, published an influential paper on the topic in 1968. Bakker specifically used anatomical and ecological evidence to argue that sauropods, which had hitherto been depicted as sprawling aquatic animals with their tails dragging on the ground, were endotherms that lived vigorous, terrestrial lives.
