In 1972, Bakker expanded on his arguments based on energy requirements and predator-prey ratios. This was one of the seminal results that led to the Dinosaur renaissance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker1968_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-bakker1968-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker1972_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-bakker1972-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor2010_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor2010-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parsons2001_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-parsons2001-172">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the greatest contributions to the modern understanding of dinosaur physiology has been <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">paleohistology</a>, the study of microscopic tissue structure in dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erickson2014_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-erickson2014-173">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailleul2019_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-bailleul2019-174">[173]</a></sup> From the 1960s forward, <a href="/wiki/Armand_de_Ricql%C3%A8s" title="Armand de Ricqlès">Armand de Ricqlès</a> suggested that the presence of fibrolamellar bone—bony tissue with an irregular, fibrous texture and filled with blood vessels—was indicative of consistently fast growth and therefore endothermy. Fibrolamellar bone was common in both dinosaurs and pterosaurs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dericqles1974_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-dericqles1974-175">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dericqles1980_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-dericqles1980-176">[175]</a></sup> though not universally present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-padian2004_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-padian2004-177">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desouza2020_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-desouza2020-178">[177]</a></sup> This has led to a significant body of work in reconstructing <a href="/wiki/Growth_curve_(biology)" title="Growth curve (biology)">growth curves</a> and modeling the evolution of growth rates across various dinosaur lineages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physiologyrefs_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-physiologyrefs-179">[178]</a></sup> which has suggested overall that dinosaurs grew faster than living reptiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bailleul2019_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-bailleul2019-174">[173]</a></sup> Other lines of evidence suggesting endothermy include the presence of feathers and other types of body coverings in many lineages (see <a href="#Feathers">§ Feathers</a>); more consistent ratios of the isotope <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-18" title="Oxygen-18">oxygen-18</a> in bony tissue compared to ectotherms, particularly as latitude and thus air temperature varied, which suggests stable internal temperatures<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amiot2006_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-amiot2006-180">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amiot2010_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-amiot2010-181">[180]</a></sup> (although these ratios can be altered during fossilization<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolodny1996_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-kolodny1996-182">[181]</a></sup>); and the discovery of <a href="/wiki/South_Polar_region_of_the_Cretaceous#Dinosaurs" title="South Polar region of the Cretaceous">polar dinosaurs</a>, which lived in Australia, Antarctica, and Alaska when these places would have had cool, temperate climates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paul1988_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-paul1988-183">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clemens1993_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-clemens1993-184">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rich2002_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-rich2002-185">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buffetaut2004_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-buffetaut2004-186">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dino_bird_h.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="629" data-file-width="999" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Dino_bird_h.jpg/220px-Dino_bird_h.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Dino_bird_h.jpg/330px-Dino_bird_h.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Dino_bird_h.jpg/440px-Dino_bird_h.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dino_bird_h.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison between the <a href="/wiki/Air_sac" title="Air sac">air sacs</a> of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abelisaur" title="Abelisaur">abelisaur</a> and a bird</div></div></div>
<p>In saurischian dinosaurs, higher metabolisms were supported by the evolution of the avian respiratory system, characterized by an extensive system of <a href="/wiki/Air_sac" title="Air sac">air sacs</a> that extended the lungs and invaded many of the bones in the skeleton, making them hollow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sereno2008_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sereno2008-187">[186]</a></sup> Such respiratory systems, which may have appeared in the earliest saurischians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oconnor2009_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-oconnor2009-188">[187]</a></sup> would have provided them with more oxygen compared to a mammal of similar size, while also having a larger resting <a href="/wiki/Tidal_volume" title="Tidal volume">tidal volume</a> and requiring a lower breathing frequency, which would have allowed them to sustain higher activity levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sander2011_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-sander2011-109">[108]</a></sup> The rapid airflow would also have been an effective cooling mechanism, which in conjunction with a lower metabolic rate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eagle2011_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-eagle2011-189">[188]</a></sup> would have prevented large sauropods from overheating. These traits may have enabled sauropods to grow quickly to gigantic sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wedel2003_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-wedel2003-190">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perry2009_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-perry2009-191">[190]</a></sup> Sauropods may also have benefitted from their size—their small surface area to volume ratio meant that they would have been able to thermoregulate more easily, a phenomenon termed <a href="/wiki/Gigantothermy" title="Gigantothermy">gigantothermy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sander2011_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-sander2011-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alexander1998_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-alexander1998-192">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like other reptiles, dinosaurs are primarily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uricotelic" title="Uricotelic">uricotelic</a>, that is, their <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> extract nitrogenous wastes from their bloodstream and excrete it as <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> via the ureters into the intestine. This would have helped them to conserve water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benson2018_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-benson2018-168">[167]</a></sup> In most living species, uric acid is excreted along with feces as a semisolid waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsahar2005_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsahar2005-193">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skadhauge2003_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-skadhauge2003-194">[193]</a></sup> However, at least some modern birds (such as <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">hummingbirds</a>) can be facultatively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonotelic" title="Ammonotelic">ammonotelic</a>, excreting most of the nitrogenous wastes as ammonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-preest1997_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-preest1997-195">[194]</a></sup> This material, as well as the output of the intestines, emerges from the <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">cloaca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mora1965_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-mora1965-196">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-packard1966_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-packard1966-197">[196]</a></sup> In addition, many species regurgitate <a href="/wiki/Pellet_(ornithology)" title="Pellet (ornithology)">pellets</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-balgooyen1971_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-balgooyen1971-198">[197]</a></sup> and fossil pellets are known as early as the Jurassic from <i><a href="/wiki/Anchiornis" title="Anchiornis">Anchiornis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xu2018_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-xu2018-199">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>The size and shape of the brain can be partly reconstructed based on the surrounding bones. In 1896, Marsh calculated ratios between brain weight and body weight of seven species of dinosaurs, showing that the brain of dinosaurs was proportionally smaller than in today's crocodiles, and that the brain of <i>Stegosaurus</i> was smaller than in any living land vertebrate. This contributed to the widespread public notion of dinosaurs as being sluggish and extraordinarily stupid. Harry Jerison, in 1973, showed that proportionally smaller brains are expected at larger body sizes, and that brain size in dinosaurs was not smaller than expected when compared to living reptiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell1997_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell1997-200">[199]</a></sup> Later research showed that relative brain size progressively increased during the evolution of theropods, with the highest intelligence – comparable to that of modern birds – calculated for the troodontid <i><a href="/wiki/Troodon" title="Troodon">Troodon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brusatte2012_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-brusatte2012-201">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_birds">Origin of birds</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_birds" title="Origin of birds">Origin of birds</a></div>
<p>The possibility that dinosaurs were the ancestors of birds was first suggested in 1868 by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huxley1868_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-huxley1868-202">[201]</a></sup> After the work of <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Heilmann" title="Gerhard Heilmann">Gerhard Heilmann</a> in the early 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_theory" title="Scientific theory">theory</a> of birds as dinosaur descendants was abandoned in favor of the idea of them being descendants of generalized <a href="/wiki/Thecodontia" title="Thecodontia">thecodonts</a>, with the key piece of evidence being the supposed lack of <a href="/wiki/Clavicle" title="Clavicle">clavicles</a> in dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heilmann_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-heilmann-203">[202]</a></sup> However, as later discoveries showed, clavicles (or a single fused <a href="/wiki/Furcula" title="Furcula">wishbone</a>, which derived from separate clavicles) were not actually absent;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-12">[11]</a></sup> they had been found as early as 1924 in <i><a href="/wiki/Oviraptor" title="Oviraptor">Oviraptor</a></i>, but misidentified as an <a href="/wiki/Interclavicle" title="Interclavicle">interclavicle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HO24_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-HO24-204">[203]</a></sup> In the 1970s, Ostrom revived the dinosaur–bird theory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ostrom1973_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-ostrom1973-205">[204]</a></sup> which gained momentum in the coming decades with the advent of cladistic analysis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gauthier1986_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-gauthier1986-206">[205]</a></sup> and a great increase in the discovery of small theropods and early birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TRHJ00_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-TRHJ00-30">[29]</a></sup> Of particular note have been the fossils of the <a href="/wiki/Yixian_Formation" title="Yixian Formation">Yixian Formation</a>, where a variety of theropods and early birds have been found, often with feathers of some type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161208_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161208-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-12">[11]</a></sup> Birds share over a hundred distinct anatomical features with theropod dinosaurs, which are now generally accepted to have been their closest ancient relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayretal2005_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayretal2005-207">[206]</a></sup> They are most closely allied with maniraptoran coelurosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-12">[11]</a></sup> A minority of scientists, most notably <a href="/wiki/Alan_Feduccia" title="Alan Feduccia">Alan Feduccia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Larry_Martin" title="Larry Martin">Larry Martin</a>, have proposed other evolutionary paths, including revised versions of Heilmann's basal archosaur proposal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martin2004_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-martin2004-208">[207]</a></sup> or that maniraptoran theropods are the ancestors of birds but themselves are not dinosaurs, only <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent</a> with dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF02_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-AF02-209">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feathers">Feathers</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaurs" title="Feathered dinosaurs">Feathered dinosaurs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2444" data-file-width="2372" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg/220px-Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg/330px-Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg/440px-Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various feathered non-avian dinosaurs, including <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeopteryx" title="Archaeopteryx">Archaeopteryx</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Anchiornis" title="Anchiornis">Anchiornis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Microraptor" title="Microraptor">Microraptor</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Zhenyuanlong" title="Zhenyuanlong">Zhenyuanlong</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Feathers are one of the most recognizable characteristics of modern birds, and a trait that was also shared by several non-avian dinosaurs. Based on the current distribution of fossil evidence, it appears that feathers were an ancestral dinosaurian trait, though one that may have been selectively lost in some species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-switeknature_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-switeknature-210">[209]</a></sup> Direct fossil evidence of feathers or feather-like structures has been discovered in a diverse array of species in many non-avian dinosaur groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161208_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161208-64">[63]</a></sup> both among saurischians and ornithischians. Simple, branched, feather-like structures are known from <a href="/wiki/Heterodontosauridae" title="Heterodontosauridae">heterodontosaurids</a>, primitive <a href="/wiki/Neornithischia" title="Neornithischia">neornithischians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Godefroit2014_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Godefroit2014-211">[210]</a></sup> and theropods,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xuetal2004_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xuetal2004-212">[211]</a></sup> and primitive ceratopsians. Evidence for true, vaned feathers similar to the flight feathers of modern birds has been found only in the theropod subgroup Maniraptora, which includes oviraptorosaurs, troodontids, dromaeosaurids, and birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_12-6"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GC06_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-GC06-213">[212]</a></sup> Feather-like structures known as <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur#Pycnofibers" title="Pterosaur">pycnofibres</a> have also been found in pterosaurs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kellneretal2009_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-kellneretal2009-214">[213]</a></sup> suggesting the possibility that feather-like filaments may have been common in the bird lineage and evolved before the appearance of dinosaurs themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-switeknature_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-switeknature-210">[209]</a></sup> Research into the genetics of <a href="/wiki/American_alligator" title="American alligator">American alligators</a> has also revealed that crocodylian <a href="/wiki/Scute" title="Scute">scutes</a> do possess feather-keratins during embryonic development, but these keratins are not expressed by the animals before hatching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlibL2006_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlibL2006-215">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Archaeopteryx" title="Archaeopteryx">Archaeopteryx</a></i> was the first fossil found that revealed a potential connection between dinosaurs and birds. It is considered a <a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">transitional fossil</a>, in that it displays features of both groups. Brought to light just two years after <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>'s seminal <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i> (1859), its discovery spurred the nascent debate between proponents of <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">evolutionary biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Creationism" title="Creationism">creationism</a>. This early bird is so dinosaur-like that, without a clear impression of feathers in the surrounding rock, at least one specimen was mistaken for the small theropod <i><a href="/wiki/Compsognathus" title="Compsognathus">Compsognathus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PW88_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-PW88-216">[215]</a></sup> Since the 1990s, a number of additional feathered dinosaurs have been found, providing even stronger evidence of the close relationship between dinosaurs and modern birds. Most of these specimens were unearthed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lagerst%C3%A4tten" title="Lagerstätten">lagerstätte</a> of the Yixian Formation, <a href="/wiki/Liaoning" title="Liaoning">Liaoning</a>, northeastern China, which was part of an island continent during the Cretaceous. Though feathers have been found in only a few locations, it is possible that non-avian dinosaurs elsewhere in the world were also feathered. The lack of widespread fossil evidence for feathered non-avian dinosaurs may be because delicate features like skin and feathers are seldom preserved by fossilization and thus often absent from the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzeretal1999_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzeretal1999-217">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>The description of feathered dinosaurs has not been without controversy; perhaps the most vocal critics have been Alan Feduccia and Theagarten Lingham-Soliar, who have proposed that some purported feather-like fossils are the result of the decomposition of collagenous fiber that underlaid the dinosaurs' skin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TLS03_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-TLS03-218">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FLH05_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FLH05-219">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSFX07_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSFX07-220">[219]</a></sup> and that maniraptoran dinosaurs with vaned feathers were not actually dinosaurs, but convergent with dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF02_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-AF02-209">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FLH05_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-FLH05-219">[218]</a></sup> However, their views have for the most part not been accepted by other researchers, to the point that the scientific nature of Feduccia's proposals has been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prum2003_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prum2003-221">[220]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skeleton">Skeleton</span></h3>
<p>Because feathers are often associated with birds, feathered dinosaurs are often touted as the missing link between birds and dinosaurs. However, the multiple skeletal features also shared by the two groups represent another important line of evidence for paleontologists. Areas of the skeleton with important similarities include the neck, pubis, <a href="/wiki/Wrist" title="Wrist">wrist</a> (semi-lunate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpal" title="Carpal">carpal</a>), arm and <a href="/wiki/Shoulder_girdle" title="Shoulder girdle">pectoral girdle</a>, furcula (wishbone), and <a href="/wiki/Keel_(bird_anatomy)" title="Keel (bird anatomy)">breast bone</a>. Comparison of bird and dinosaur skeletons through cladistic analysis strengthens the case for the link.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archaeopteryxucmp_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-archaeopteryxucmp-222">[221]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soft_anatomy">Soft anatomy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2342" data-file-width="1876" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg/220px-Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg/330px-Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg/440px-Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pneumatopores on the left <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">ilium</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Aerosteon" title="Aerosteon">Aerosteon riocoloradensis</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Large meat-eating dinosaurs had a complex system of air sacs similar to those found in modern birds, according to a 2005 investigation led by Patrick M. O'Connor. The lungs of theropod dinosaurs (carnivores that walked on two legs and had bird-like feet) likely pumped air into hollow sacs in their skeletons, as is the case in birds. "What was once formally considered unique to birds was present in some form in the ancestors of birds", O'Connor said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OConnorClaessens2005_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-OConnorClaessens2005-223">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[223]</a></sup> In 2008, scientists described <i><a href="/wiki/Aerosteon" title="Aerosteon">Aerosteon riocoloradensis</a></i>, the skeleton of which supplies the strongest evidence to date of a dinosaur with a bird-like breathing system. <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scanning</a> of <i>Aerosteon'</i>s fossil bones revealed evidence for the existence of air sacs within the animal's body cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sereno2008_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sereno2008-187">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newswise2_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-newswise2-225">[224]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_evidence">Behavioral evidence</span></h3>
<p>Fossils of the troodonts <i><a href="/wiki/Mei_long" title="Mei long">Mei</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sinornithoides" title="Sinornithoides">Sinornithoides</a></i> demonstrate that some dinosaurs slept with their heads tucked under their arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XUNorell2004_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-XUNorell2004-226">[225]</a></sup> This behavior, which may have helped to keep the head warm, is also characteristic of modern birds. Several <a href="/wiki/Deinonychosauria" title="Deinonychosauria">deinonychosaur</a> and oviraptorosaur specimens have also been found preserved on top of their nests, likely brooding in a bird-like manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-norell1995_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-norell1995-227">[226]</a></sup> The ratio between egg volume and body mass of adults among these dinosaurs suggest that the eggs were primarily brooded by the male, and that the young were highly precocial, similar to many modern ground-dwelling birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varricchioetal2008_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Varricchioetal2008-228">[227]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some dinosaurs are known to have used <a href="/wiki/Gizzard" title="Gizzard">gizzard</a> stones like modern birds. These stones are swallowed by animals to aid digestion and break down food and hard fibers once they enter the stomach. When found in association with fossils, gizzard stones are called gastroliths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wings2007_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-wings2007-229">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extinction_of_major_groups">Extinction of major groups</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a></div>
<p>All non-avian dinosaurs and most lineages of birds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longrich2011_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-longrich2011-230">[229]</a></sup> became extinct in a <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">mass extinction event</a>, called the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene (K-Pg) extinction event</a>, at the end of the Cretaceous period. Above the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_boundary" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary">Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary</a>, which has been dated to 66.038 ± 0.025 million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renne2013_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-renne2013-231">[230]</a></sup> fossils of non-avian dinosaurs disappear abruptly; the absence of dinosaur fossils was historically used to assign rocks to the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a>. The nature of the event that caused this mass extinction has been extensively studied since the 1970s, leading to the development of two mechanisms that are thought to have played major roles: an extraterrestrial <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">impact event</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Flood_basalt" title="Flood basalt">flood basalt</a> volcanism in India. However, the specific mechanisms of the extinction event and the extent of its effects on dinosaurs are still areas of ongoing research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brusatte2014_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-brusatte2014-232">[231]</a></sup> Alongside dinosaurs, many other groups of animals became extinct: pterosaurs, marine reptiles such as mosasaurs and plesiosaurs, several groups of mammals, <a href="/wiki/Ammonoidea" title="Ammonoidea">ammonites</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">nautilus</a>-like <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">mollusks</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rudist" title="Rudist">rudists</a> (<a href="/wiki/Reef" title="Reef">reef</a>-building <a href="/wiki/Bivalvia" title="Bivalvia">bivalves</a>), and various groups of marine plankton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macleod1997_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-macleod1997-233">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archibald1982_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-archibald1982-234">[233]</a></sup> In all, approximately 47% of genera and 76% of species on Earth became extinct during the K-Pg extinction event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jablonski1991_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-jablonski1991-235">[234]</a></sup> The relatively large size of most dinosaurs and the low diversity of small-bodied dinosaur species at the end of the Cretaceous may have contributed to their extinction;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[235]</a></sup> the extinction of the bird lineages that did not survive may also have been caused by a dependence on forest habitats or a lack of adaptations to <a href="/wiki/Seed_predation" title="Seed predation">eating seeds</a> for survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-field2018_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-field2018-237">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2016_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-larson2016-238">[237]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-extinction_diversity">Pre-extinction diversity</span></h3>
<p>Just before the K-Pg extinction event, the number of non-avian dinosaur species that existed globally has been estimated at between 628 and 1078.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leloeuff2012_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-leloeuff2012-239">[238]</a></sup> It remains uncertain whether the diversity of dinosaurs was in gradual decline before the K-Pg extinction event, or whether dinosaurs were actually thriving prior to the extinction. Rock formations from the <a href="/wiki/Maastrichtian" title="Maastrichtian">Maastrichtian</a> epoch, which directly preceded the extinction, have been found to have lower diversity than the preceding <a href="/wiki/Campanian" title="Campanian">Campanian</a> epoch, which led to the prevailing view of a long-term decline in diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macleod1997_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-macleod1997-233">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archibald1982_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-archibald1982-234">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpenter1983_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-carpenter1983-240">[239]</a></sup> However, these comparisons did not account either for varying preservation potential between rock units or for different extents of exploration and excavation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brusatte2014_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-brusatte2014-232">[231]</a></sup> In 1984, <a href="/wiki/Dale_Russell" title="Dale Russell">Dale Russell</a> carried out an analysis to account for these biases, and found no evidence of a decline;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell1984_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell1984-241">[240]</a></sup> another analysis by David Fastovsky and colleagues in 2004 even showed that dinosaur diversity continually increased until the extinction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fastovsky2004_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-fastovsky2004-242">[241]</a></sup> but this analysis has been rebutted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sullivan2006_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-sullivan2006-243">[242]</a></sup> Since then, different approaches based on statistics and mathematical models have variously supported either a sudden extinction<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brusatte2014_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-brusatte2014-232">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leloeuff2012_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-leloeuff2012-239">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiarenza2019_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-chiarenza2019-244">[243]</a></sup> or a gradual decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lloyd2011_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-lloyd2011-245">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sakamoto2016_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-sakamoto2016-246">[245]</a></sup> End-Cretaceous trends in diversity may have varied between dinosaur lineages: it has been suggested that sauropods were not in decline, while ornithischians and theropods were in decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrett2009_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-barrett2009-247">[246]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-upchurch2011_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-upchurch2011-248">[247]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_event">Impact event</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_crater" title="Chicxulub crater">Chicxulub crater</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LWA_with_Walt.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="1566" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/LWA_with_Walt.JPG/170px-LWA_with_Walt.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/LWA_with_Walt.JPG/255px-LWA_with_Walt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/LWA_with_Walt.JPG/340px-LWA_with_Walt.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LWA_with_Walt.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">Luis</a> (left) and his son <a href="/wiki/Walter_Alvarez" title="Walter Alvarez">Walter Alvarez</a> (right) at the K-T Boundary in <a href="/wiki/Gubbio" title="Gubbio">Gubbio</a>, Italy, 1981</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="799" data-file-width="715" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg/220px-Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg/330px-Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg/440px-Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicxulub_Crater" title="Chicxulub Crater">Chicxulub Crater</a> at the tip of the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a>; the impactor that formed this crater may have caused the dinosaur <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Alvarez_hypothesis" title="Alvarez hypothesis">bolide impact hypothesis</a>, first brought to wide attention in 1980 by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Alvarez" title="Walter Alvarez">Walter Alvarez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">Luis Alvarez</a>, and colleagues, attributes the K-Pg extinction event to a <a href="/wiki/Bolide" title="Bolide">bolide</a> (extraterrestrial projectile) impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[248]</a></sup> Alvarez and colleagues proposed that a sudden increase in <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a> levels, recorded around the world in rock deposits at the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary, was direct evidence of the impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alvarez1980_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-alvarez1980-250">[249]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shocked_quartz" title="Shocked quartz">Shocked quartz</a>, indicative of a strong shockwave emanating from an impact, was also found worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bohor1987_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-bohor1987-251">[250]</a></sup> The actual impact site remained elusive until a <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_crater" title="Chicxulub crater">crater</a> measuring 180 km (110 mi) wide was discovered in the Yucatán Peninsula of southeastern <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, and was publicized in a 1991 paper by <a href="/wiki/Alan_R._Hildebrand" title="Alan R. Hildebrand">Alan Hildebrand</a> and colleagues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hildebrand1991_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-hildebrand1991-252">[251]</a></sup> Now, the bulk of the evidence suggests that a bolide <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>5 to 15 kilometers (3 to <span class="frac" role="math">9<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> miles) wide impacted the Yucatán Peninsula 66 million years ago, forming this crater<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pope1996_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-pope1996-253">[252]</a></sup> and creating a "kill mechanism" that triggered the extinction event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schulte2010_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-schulte2010-254">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kring2007_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-kring2007-255">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiarenza2020_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-chiarenza2020-256">[255]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within hours, the Chicxulub impact would have created immediate effects such as earthquakes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ivanov2005_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-ivanov2005-257">[256]</a></sup> tsunamis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-matsui2002_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-matsui2002-258">[257]</a></sup> and a global firestorm that likely killed unsheltered animals and started wildfires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robertson2004_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-robertson2004-259">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robertson2013_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-robertson2013-260">[259]</a></sup> However, it would also have had longer-term consequences for the environment. Within days, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphate" title="Sulphate">sulphate</a> <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosols</a> released from rocks at the impact site would have contributed to <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pope1997_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-pope1997-261">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ohno2014_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-ohno2014-262">[261]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">Soot</a> aerosols are thought to have spread around the world over the ensuing months and years; they would have cooled the surface of the Earth by reflecting <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a>, and greatly slowed <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> by blocking out sunlight, thus creating an <a href="/wiki/Impact_winter" title="Impact winter">impact winter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brusatte2014_232-3"><a href="#cite_note-brusatte2014-232">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaiho2016_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-kaiho2016-263">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lyons2020_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-lyons2020-264">[263]</a></sup> (This role was ascribed to sulphate aerosols until experiments demonstrated otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ohno2014_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-ohno2014-262">[261]</a></sup>) The cessation of photosynthesis would have led to the collapse of <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food webs</a> depending on leafy plants, which included all dinosaurs save for grain-eating birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2016_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-larson2016-238">[237]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deccan_Traps">Deccan Traps</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a></div>
<p>At the time of the K-Pg extinction, the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a> flood basalts of India were actively erupting. The eruptions can be separated into three phases around the K-Pg boundary, two prior to the boundary and one after. The second phase, which occurred very close to the boundary, would have extruded 70 to 80% of the volume of these eruptions in intermittent pulses that occurred around 100,000 years apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chenet2009_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-chenet2009-265">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schoene2019_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-schoene2019-266">[265]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gases">Greenhouse gases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphur_dioxide" title="Sulphur dioxide">sulphur dioxide</a> would have been released by this volcanic activity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclean1985_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-mclean1985-267">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-self2006_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-self2006-268">[267]</a></sup> resulting in <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> through temperature perturbations of roughly 3 °C (5.4 °F) but possibly as high as 7 °C (13 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tobin2017_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-tobin2017-269">[268]</a></sup> Like the Chicxulub impact, the eruptions may also have released sulphate aerosols, which would have caused acid rain and global cooling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmidt2016_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-schmidt2016-270">[269]</a></sup> However, due to large error margins in the dating of the eruptions, the role of the Deccan Traps in the K-Pg extinction remains unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renne2013_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-renne2013-231">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brusatte2014_232-4"><a href="#cite_note-brusatte2014-232">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hofman2000_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-hofman2000-271">[270]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before 2000, arguments that the Deccan Traps eruptions—as opposed to the Chicxulub impact—caused the extinction were usually linked to the view that the extinction was gradual. Prior to the discovery of the Chicxulub crater, the Deccan Traps were used to explain the global iridium layer;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclean1985_267-1"><a href="#cite_note-mclean1985-267">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sahni1988_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-sahni1988-272">[271]</a></sup> even after the crater's discovery, the impact was still thought to only have had a regional, not global, effect on the extinction event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glasby1996_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-glasby1996-273">[272]</a></sup> In response, Luis Alvarez rejected volcanic activity as an explanation for the iridium layer and the extinction as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alvarez1987_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-alvarez1987-274">[273]</a></sup> Since then, however, most researchers have adopted a more moderate position, which identifies the Chicxulub impact as the primary progenitor of the extinction while also recognizing that the Deccan Traps may also have played a role. Walter Alvarez himself has acknowledged that the Deccan Traps and other ecological factors may have contributed to the extinctions in addition to the Chicxulub impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alvarez1997_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-alvarez1997-275">[274]</a></sup> Some estimates have placed the start of the second phase in the Deccan Traps eruptions within 50,000 years after the Chicxulub impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renne2015_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-renne2015-276">[275]</a></sup> Combined with mathematical modelling of the <a href="/wiki/Seismic_wave" title="Seismic wave">seismic waves</a> that would have been generated by the impact, this has led to the suggestion that the Chicxulub impact may have triggered these eruptions by increasing the permeability of the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_plume" title="Mantle plume">mantle plume</a> underlying the Deccan Traps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richards2015_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-richards2015-277">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khazins2019_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-khazins2019-278">[277]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whether the Deccan Traps were a major cause of the extinction, on par with the Chicxulub impact, remains uncertain. Proponents consider the climatic impact of the sulphur dioxide released to have been on par with the Chicxulub impact, and also note the role of flood basalt volcanism in other mass extinctions like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permian-Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian-Triassic extinction event">Permian-Triassic extinction event</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archibald2010_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-archibald2010-279">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-courtillot2010_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-courtillot2010-280">[279]</a></sup> They consider the Chicxulub impact to have worsened the ongoing climate change caused by the eruptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keller2014_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-keller2014-281">[280]</a></sup> Meanwhile, detractors point out the sudden nature of the extinction and that other pulses in Deccan Traps activity of comparable magnitude did not appear to have caused extinctions. They also contend that the causes of different mass extinctions should be assessed separately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schulte2010reply_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-schulte2010reply-282">[281]</a></sup> In 2020, Alfio Chiarenza and colleagues suggested that the Deccan Traps may even have had the opposite effect: they suggested that the long-term warming caused by its carbon dioxide emissions may have dampened the impact winter from the Chicxulub impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiarenza2020_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-chiarenza2020-256">[255]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_Paleocene_survivors">Possible Paleocene survivors</span></h3>
<p>Non-avian dinosaur remains have occasionally been found above the K-Pg boundary. In 2000, <a href="/wiki/Spencer_G._Lucas" title="Spencer G. Lucas">Spencer Lucas</a> and colleagues reported the discovery of a single hadrosaur right femur in the <a href="/wiki/San_Juan_Basin" title="San Juan Basin">San Juan Basin</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, and described it as evidence of Paleocene dinosaurs. The rock unit in which the bone was discovered has been dated to the early <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a> epoch, approximately 64.8 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fassett2011_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-fassett2011-283">[282]</a></sup> If the bone was not <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">re-deposited</a> by weathering action, it would provide evidence that some dinosaur populations may have survived at least half a million years into the Cenozoic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fassett2009_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-fassett2009-284">[283]</a></sup> Other evidence includes the presence of dinosaur remains in the Hell Creek Formation up to 1.3 m (4.3 ft) above the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary, representing 40,000 years of elapsed time. This has been used to support the view that the K-Pg extinction was gradual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sloan1986_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-sloan1986-285">[284]</a></sup> However, these supposed Paleocene dinosaurs are considered by many other researchers to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reworked_fossil" title="Reworked fossil">reworked</a>, that is, washed out of their original locations and then re-buried in younger sediments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lucas2009_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-lucas2009-286">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renne2012_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-renne2012-287">[286]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lofgren1990_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-lofgren1990-288">[287]</a></sup> The age estimates have also been considered unreliable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koenig2012_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-koenig2012-289">[288]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_depictions">Cultural depictions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_dinosaurs" title="Cultural depictions of dinosaurs">Cultural depictions of dinosaurs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="704" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg/220px-Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg/330px-Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg/440px-Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Outdated <i>Iguanodon</i> statues created by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Waterhouse_Hawkins" title="Benjamin Waterhouse Hawkins">Benjamin Waterhouse Hawkins</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_Park" title="Crystal Palace Park">Crystal Palace Park</a> in 1853</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg/220px-Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg/330px-Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg/440px-Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The battles that may have occurred between <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops</a></i> are a recurring theme in <a href="/wiki/Popular_science" title="Popular science">popular science</a> and dinosaurs' depiction in culture</div></div></div>
<p>By human standards, dinosaurs were creatures of fantastic appearance and often enormous size. As such, they have captured the popular imagination and become an enduring part of human culture. The entry of the word "dinosaur" into the common <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> reflects the animals' cultural importance: in English, "dinosaur" is commonly used to describe anything that is impractically large, obsolete, or bound for extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m-w_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-m-w-290">[289]</a></sup>
</p><p>Public enthusiasm for dinosaurs first developed in <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a> England, where in 1854, three decades after the first scientific descriptions of dinosaur remains, a menagerie of lifelike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_dinosaurs" title="Crystal Palace dinosaurs">dinosaur sculptures</a> was unveiled in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_Park" title="Crystal Palace Park">Crystal Palace Park</a>. The Crystal Palace dinosaurs proved so popular that a strong market in smaller replicas soon developed. In subsequent decades, dinosaur exhibits opened at parks and <a href="/wiki/Natural_history_museum" title="Natural history museum">museums</a> around the world, ensuring that successive generations would be introduced to the animals in an immersive and exciting way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-torrens1993_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-torrens1993-291">[290]</a></sup> The enduring popularity of dinosaurs, in its turn, has resulted in significant public funding for dinosaur science, and has frequently spurred new discoveries. In the United States, for example, the competition between museums for public attention led directly to the Bone Wars of the 1880s and 1890s, during which a pair of feuding paleontologists made enormous scientific contributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-breithaupt1997_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-breithaupt1997-292">[291]</a></sup>
</p><p>The popular preoccupation with dinosaurs has ensured their appearance in <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">film</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">media</a>. Beginning in 1852 with a passing mention in <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Bleak_House" title="Bleak House">Bleak House</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bleakhouse_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-bleakhouse-293">[292]</a></sup> dinosaurs have been featured in large numbers of <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fictional</a> works. <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>'s 1864 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_Center_of_the_Earth" title="Journey to the Center of the Earth">Journey to the Center of the Earth</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle">Sir Arthur Conan Doyle</a>'s 1912 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_World_(Doyle_novel)" title="The Lost World (Doyle novel)">The Lost World</a></i>, the 1914 animated film <i><a href="/wiki/Gertie_the_Dinosaur" title="Gertie the Dinosaur">Gertie the Dinosaur</a></i> (featuring the first animated dinosaur), the iconic 1933 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motion_picture" title="Motion picture">film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/King_Kong_(1933_film)" title="King Kong (1933 film)">King Kong</a></i>, the 1954 <i><a href="/wiki/Godzilla_(1954_film)" title="Godzilla (1954 film)">Godzilla</a></i> and its many sequels, the best-selling 1990 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(novel)" title="Jurassic Park (novel)">Jurassic Park</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Crichton" title="Michael Crichton">Michael Crichton</a> and its 1993 <a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(film)" title="Jurassic Park (film)">film adaptation</a> are just a few notable examples of dinosaur appearances in fiction. Authors of general-interest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-fiction" title="Non-fiction">non-fiction</a> works about dinosaurs, including some prominent paleontologists, have often sought to use the animals as a way to educate readers about science in general. Dinosaurs are ubiquitous in <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a>; numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Company_(law)" title="Company (law)">companies</a> have referenced dinosaurs in printed or televised advertisements, either in order to sell their own products or in order to characterize their rivals as slow-moving, dim-witted, or obsolete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFGlut1997_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-DFGlut1997-294">[293]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[294]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-cold-blooded-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-cold-blooded_7-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dinosaurs (including birds) are members of the <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">natural group</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reptilia" title="Reptilia">Reptilia</a>. Their biology does not precisely correspond to the antiquated <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a> Reptilia of <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_taxonomy" title="Linnaean taxonomy">Linnaean taxonomy</a>, consisting of <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">cold-blooded</a> <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotes</a> without fur or feathers. As Linnean taxonomy was formulated for modern animals prior to the study of evolution and paleontology, it fails to account for extinct animals with intermediate traits between traditional classes.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
